A. 
Program Purpose and Project Development Objective 
1. 
Program 
purpose 
and 
program 
phasing: 
The 
purpose 
of 
the 
Bahia 
Health 
System 
Reform 
Project 
is 
to 
assist 
the 
Government 
of 
Bahia 
in 
its 
efforts 
to improve 
the 
health 
status 
of 
the 
Bahian 
population 
and 
to 
improve 
access, 
equity 
and 
efficiency 
in 
the 
health 
sector. 
The 
program 
design 
is 
based 
on 
the 
national policies 
for 
the decentralization 
of 
health 
care 
and 
on 
the 
state 
policies for 
poverty 
alleviation. 
These 
policies 
were 
reviewed 
by 
the 
Bank 
on 
a 
study 
(“Decentralization 
of 
Health 
Care 
in 
Brazil 
- 
A 
Case 
study 
of 
Bahia); 
the 
main 
recommendations 
of 
the 
study 
have been 
discussed 
with 
the 
incoming 
Governor 
of 
Bahia 
and 
are 
incorporated 
into 
the 
program. 
The program 
is 
described 
in 
the 
Letter 
of 
Development 
Policy 
(Annex 
1A). 
The 
Program 
complements 
numerous Government 
actions 
in 
the 
health 
sector 
and 
in 
other 
sectors 
with 
the potential 
to 
improve 
the 
health 
of 
the 
population (including 
education, water 
and 
sanitation 
and 
others). 
The 
integration 
of 
the 
Program 
with 
other 
Government 
actions 
is 
described 
in 
the 
Bahia 
State 
Assistance Strategy 
(Annex 
12 
of 
the 
PAD).  The 
program 
is 
designed 
as 
a seven-year 
effort, 
with 
a 
first 
phase 
(APL1: 
4 
years) 
of 
US$50 
million 
and 
a second 
phase 
(APL2: 
3 
years) 
of 
US$50 
million. 
Activities 
planned 
to 
be 
financed 
by 
APL2 
are a 
natural 
continuation 
of 
those 
financed 
under 
APL1. 
2. 
Project 
development objective: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
The 
development 
objectives 
of 
the 
Bahia 
Health 
System 
Reform 
Project 
are 
to 
implement 
the 
national 
policy 
of 
health 
care 
decentralization 
seeking 
to: 
(i) 
improve 
access 
of 
the 
poor 
to 
Basic 
Health 
Care, 
as 
measured 
by 
10 
Performance 
Indicators 
that 
measure 
the coverage 
and 
quality 
of 
the 
health 
interventions 
with 
the 
greatest 
influence  over 
child 
mortality, 
maternal 
mortality,  and 
the  incidence 
of 
diseases 
prevalent 
among 
the 
poor; 
(ii) 
reduce 
inequities 
in 
the 
allocation 
of 
publicly 
funded 
health 
care 
services, 
as 
measured 
by 
differentials  between per-capita 
regional 
budgets; 
and 
(iii) 
improve 
the eflciency  and 
quality 
in 
the 
use 
ofpublic 
resources, 
as 
measured 
by 
the 
implementation 
of 
regulation 
systems 
mandated 
by 
national 
policies. 
Hospital 
admissions 
due 
to 
diarrheal 
infections 
Admissions 
due 
to 
respiratory 
infections 
for 
3. 
Key 
performance 
indicators: 
(see 
Annex 
1)

the 
end 
of 
the 
program 
in 
2010, 
the 
following 
results 
are expected: 
2

Page  8
Information 
on 
health 
outcomes 
is 
produced regularly  and 
with 
an  internationally 
recognized 
methodology; 
The 
proportion 
of 
federal 
and 
state 
spending concentrated 
in 
Salvador’s 
population 
is 
reduced 
from 
40% 
to 
30% 
of 
the total; 
Multi-municipal 
health 
micro-regions 
operate 
as 
purchasers 
of 
services, 
audit 
the 
provision 
of 
care 
and 
are 
accountable 
in 
the 
way 
they 
provide 
access 
to 
health 
care 
to 
the 
municipalities 
in 
their 
area; 
High 
complexity 
care 
is 
regulated 
with 
transparent 
rules 
for 
the 
allocation 
of 
resources 
to 
interventions 
and 
to 
patients 
and 
high 
complexity providers 
are 
subject 
to 
audits; 
The 
role 
of 
the 
State 
Health 
Secretariat 
as 
a 
provider 
of 
services 
is 
reduced 
by 
limiting 
the 
number 
of 
small 
hospitals  under 
its 
direct  control 
to 
no 
more 
that 
5, 
by 
transferring 
the 
responsibility 
for 
management 
of 
secondary 
health 
services 
in 
Salvador 
to 
the 
municipal 
government, 
and 
by 
providing 
new 
forms 
of 
autonomous management 
to 
the 
tertiary-level 
referral 
hospitals. 
B. 
Strategic Context 
1. 
Sector-related 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy (CAS) 
goal 
supported 
by 
the 
project: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Document number: 
24182-BR 
Date 
of 
latest 
CAS 
discussion: 
March 
6,2000; 
CAS 
Update 
May 
20,2002 
The 
overall 
CAS 
goal 
is 
to 
promote 
sustainable 
long-term 
growth,  alleviate 
poverty 
and 
reduce 
income 
inequality 
in 
Brazil, 
while 
the  specific 
health 
sector 
goals 
of 
the 
CAS 
are 
to 
reduce 
child 
mortality, 
improve 
maternal 
health 
and combat 
HN/AIDS 
and 
other 
diseases. 
The 
CAS 
update 
report 
of 
May 
2002 
concluded  that 
CAS 
goals 
were 
being 
implemented effectively 
and  the 
goals 
remain 
appropriate 
- 
specifically, 
infant mortality 
fell 
from 
47.8 
per 
thousand 
in 
1990 
to 
29.6 
per 
thousand 
in 
2000. 
A 
new 
CAS 
will 
be 
discussed 
with 
the 
new 
government that 
took power 
in 
January 
2003 
and 
it 
is 
expected 
to 
lay 
out 
how 
Bank 
assistance 
would 
help 
Brazil 
achieve 
a 
set 
of 
social 
indicator 
targets, 
properly 
adapted 
from 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals 
(MDGs). 
The 
proposed 
Bahia 
Health 
System 
Reform 
Project 
is 
in 
line 
with 
CAS 
goals 
in 
its 
emphasis 
on 
improving 
health 
outcomes 
and 
providing 
expanded 
access 
to 
Basic 
Care 
and reducing 
health 
inequalities. 
The 
CAS 
update  emphasized 
the 
role 
of 
the 
new 
operational 
norm 
of 
2001 
(NOAS) 
on 
health 
decentralization, 
and 
this 
program 
supports 
implementation 
of 
the 
policies reflected 
in 
that 
norm. 
The 
program 
also 
adheres 
to 
CAS 
goals 
in 
its 
geographic focus 
on 
Bahia 
-- 
a 
poor 
state 
in 
the Northeast, one 
of 
Brazil’s 
least 
developed regions. 
Bahia 
is 
home to 
an 
estimated 
four 
million 
people 
living 
below 
the 
poverty 
line. 
The 
program 
is 
also 
in 
line 
with 
the 
integrated 
development  approach 
proposed 
by 
the 
Bank 
and 
coordinated 
with 
the 
State 
government. 
This 
approach 
seeks 
to 
integrate 
all 
Bank 
projects 
in 
Bahia, 
regardless 
of 
sector, 
under 
a 
single  coordinated 
vision 
centered 
on 
four 
key 
lines 
of 
action: 
fighting 
poverty, 
improving 
governance, 
increasing 
competitiveness, 
and 
preserving the environment. Workshops 
held 
in 
October 
and 
December 
2002 
gathered 
government 
stakeholders 
from 
each 
of 
the 
State Secretariats 
to 
discuss 
integration 
across 
sectors 
along 
these 
lines 
of 
action. 
From 
these 
meetings 
it 
was 
clear that 
projects 
in 
the 
human 
development 
sector 
(education 
and 
health) 
correspond 
to 
the 
priorities 
of 
the 
state. 
This 
project 
will 
assist 
in 
furthering 
the 
development 
of 
the 
state’s 
integrated 
approach 
by 
devoting 
funds 
of 
institutional 
development 
to 
activities 
targeted 
at 
sharpening the state-level 
integrated 
strategy and 
for 
activities 
to 
disseminate 
lessons 
from 
the 
Bahia 
experience. 
A 
central 
pillar 
of 
such 
coordinated 
approach 
to 
development 
has 
been 
called 
“Gente 
da 
Bahia” 
and 
it 
focuses 
on 
combating 
poverty 
and inequality 
by 
placing 
emphasis 
on 
education, culture,  health, 
public 
safety, 
and 
access 
to 
water 
and 
sanitation. 
This 
project, 
and 
a 
simultaneous 
education 
project 
being 
prepared, 
are 
at the 
heart 
of 
the 
Gente 
da 
Bahia 
strategy 
and 
respond to the 
key 
interest 
of 
the 
state 
of 
8 
Page  9
improving 
quality 
of 
schooling. 
In 
addition, 
through 
this 
project 
the 
state 
is 
raising 
the  capacity 
in 
municipalities 
to 
increasingly 
assume 
responsibility 
for 
basic, 
primary 
education 
(grades 
1-4). 
2. 
Main 
sector 
issues 
and 
Government 
strategy: 
Main 
Sector 
Tssues 
This 
section 
summarizes 
the 
findings 
of 
the 
Bank 
Report 
“Decentralization 
of 
Health 
Care 
in 
Brazil 
- 
A 
Case 
Study 
of 
Bahia. 
While 
Bahia, 
(and 
more 
generally 
Brazil) 
have obtained 
significant 
improvements 
in 
the 
health 
status 
of 
the 
population 
in 
the 
last 
decade, 
key 
indicators such 
as 
infant 
mortality, 
maternal 
mortality 
and 
the  incidence 
of 
diseases 
typical 
of 
the 
poor 
remains 
high. 
Brazil 
has  a 
complex 
and 
sophisticated 
health 
sector 
that 
must 
respond 
to 
the 
basic demands (such 
as 
those measured 
by 
the 
MDGs) 
and 
to 
higher 
level 
demands 
typical 
of 
a 
middle 
income country. 
This 
section 
describes 
key 
aspects 
of 
the 
health 
system 
that 
must 
be 
understood 
to 
place 
the 
Govemment’s 
strategy 
in 
context. 
The 
efforts 
to 
respond 
to 
the 
most 
basic 
needs 
are 
then 
described 
as 
part 
of 
the 
wider 
strategy. 
Decentralization 
In 
an 
effort to improve 
access 
and 
equity, 
the 
Constitution 
of 
1988 
proclaimed 
a 
universal 
right 
to 
health 
care, 
unified 
the 
health 
services 
of 
the 
social 
security 
with 
those 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
(creating the 
Sistema 
Unico 
de 
SaGde 
or 
Unified 
Health 
System 
- 
SUS), 
and 
emphasized 
a 
role 
for 
municipalities 
in 
the 
management 
of 
health 
care. 
These 
principles 
were 
implemented through  legislation 
and  regulations 
(known 
by 
their 
Portuguese 
acronym 
as 
NOBs 
-Normas 
Operacionais Basicas) 
issued 
in 
the 
1990s. 
The 
NOBs 
introduced 
two 
reforms 
that 
had 
a 
significant 
impact 
over 
access 
to 
Basic 
Care 
and 
that began 
to 
improve 
the large 
existing 
inequities that 
characterize 
the 
Brazilian 
health 
system. 
The 
first 
reform 
consisted 
of 
the 
municipalization 
of 
Basic 
Care 
with 
the 
introduction 
of 
PAB 
(Piso 
Assistencial 
Bhico), 
a 
basic package 
of 
services 
that 
must 
be 
provided 
by 
municipalities 
to 
their population 
and 
is 
financed 
by 
the  federal 
government 
through 
a 
fixed 
payment 
per 
capita 
(“capitation”). 
Participating municipalities 
must 
qualify 
for 
these 
transfers 
by 
meeting criteria 
that 
include 
signing 
of 
a 
Pacto 
de 
AtenqZo 
Bcisica 
(Basic 
Health 
Care  Agreement) 
with 
annual 
targets 
for 
the 
coverage 
of 
Basic 
Care 
programs 
in 
the 
municipality. 
The 
second 
reform 
consists 
of 
granting 
qualifying 
municipalities 
GestZo 
Plena 
do 
Sistema 
Municipal, 
a 
status 
that  entitles 
municipalities 
to 
receive 
block 
transfers 
directly 
from 
the  federal 
government 
to 
finance 
all 
levels 
of 
care 
for 
their 
population. 
For 
municipalities 
in 
this 
status, 
the 
state 
no 
longer 
channels 
the 
federal 
transfers 
(as 
it 
does elsewhere). 
While 
the 
municipalization 
of 
Basic 
Care 
is 
now 
virtually 
universal 
and 
is 
generally 
judged 
to 
be 
irreversible and 
successful, 
the 
municipalization 
of 
complex 
care 
covers 
a 
small fraction 
of 
municipalities 
and remains controversial 
on 
efficiency 
grounds. 
There 
is 
a 
growing 
concern that decentralizing 
Complex 
Care 
to 
municipalities 
is 
creating 
inefficiencies 
due 
to: 
(i) 
a 
loss 
of 
the economies 
of 
scale 
that characterize the 
production 
of 
most complex medical 
services;’ 
(ii) 
a 
reduction 
in 
the 
size 
of 
the 
risk-pool 
used 
to 
finance 
complex 
care; 
and 
(iii) 
a 
large 
increase 
in 
transaction 
costs associated 
with 
the 
need 
to 
replicate administrative 
functions 
in 
all 
municipalities. 
In 
order 
to 
retain 
the  benefits 
of 
devolving 
decision-making 
power 
to 
municipalities 
and 
to 
overcome 
the 
problems 
of 
excessive  fragmentation,  the 
Federal 
Govemment 
and  the 
Govemment 
of 
Bahia 
are 
supporting 
the 
creation 
of 
multi-municipal 
health 
micro-regions 
(described 
below). 
Financing 
of 
health 
care 
and 
inequality 
‘ 
This 
is 
not 
a 
significant 
problem 
for 
the 
provision 
of 
Basic 
Care, 
which 
can usually 
be 
efficiently 
provided 
at a 
lower 
scale. 
9 
Page  10
The 
history 
of 
SUS 
includes 
a 
series 
of 
attempts 
to 
achieve 
sustainability 
by 
introducing 
cost 
controls 
in 
health 
expenditures 
and 
by 
attempting 
to 
secure greater 
financing 
from 
sub-national 
governments. 
Upon 
its 
creation, 
SUS 
allocations 
to 
each 
provider 
in 
the 
country 
were decided 
by 
the 
federal government 
and 
costs 
were 
kept 
under 
control 
by 
allowing inflation 
to 
erode 
the 
value 
of 
the 
fee 
schedule 
used 
to 
reimburse 
services 
(the 
tabela 
SUS). 
A 
fiscal 
crisis 
in 
1993 gave 
the 
impetus 
for 
the 
introduction 
of 
an 
explicit 
system 
of 
cost 
controls 
that consisted 
of 
dividing 
the 
federal 
health 
budget 
into 
state 
budgets 
and 
giving 
state 
governments 
the 
authority 
to 
allocate 
these 
budgets. 
The 
granting 
of 
budgetary 
power to 
state 
governments 
was 
conceived 
as 
a 
transitional 
phase, 
as 
the 
constitution 
gives 
this 
responsibility 
to 
municipal 
governments. 
State 
governments 
maintained--and 
sometimes 
increased--the 
concentration 
of 
federally 
funded 
health 
expenditures 
in 
few 
geographic 
locations 
within 
the 
state 
and 
within 
these 
locations allocated 
the 
funds 
to 
individual 
providers 
without 
the 
benefit 
of 
transparent 
allocation 
rules. 
Since 
1996, 
there 
have  been 
attempts 
to 
shift 
the 
authority 
over 
federal 
resources 
from 
the 
state 
governments 
to 
municipalities. 
Since 
2001 
there 
is 
an 
additional 
attempt 
to 
create 
multi-municipal 
health 
microregions 
that 
would 
manage 
part 
of 
these 
resources. 
On 
the revenue 
side 
and 
following 
on 
the 
steps 
of 
the 
education 
sector, 
in 
2000 
Congress 
approved 
Constitutional Amendment 29 
requiring 
states 
and 
municipalities 
to 
earmark 
respectively 
12% 
and 
15% 
of 
their 
revenues 
for 
health. 
[PCl] 
In 
2000, 
Bahia 
spent 
the 
equivalent 
of 
6.7% 
of 
the 
state 
GDP 
on 
health 
care. 
The 
public 
sector finances 
45% 
of 
this 
expenditure. 
The 
Federal Government 
was 
the largest 
contributor, 
with 
57% 
of 
the 
total; 
its 
transfers 
were 
earmarked 
to 
Basic 
Care 
(27%), 
medium 
complexity 
care 
(63%) 
and 
high 
complexity 
care 
(10%). 
Federal 
transfers 
for 
Basic 
Care 
go directly 
to 
municipalities 
under 
the 
PAB 
system. 
Nineteen 
of 
the 417 
municipalities 
of 
the 
state 
have 
qualified for 
Gestdo 
Plena 
do Sistema 
Municipal, 
and 
receive 
9% 
of 
the 
federal transfers 
to Bahia 
for 
Medium 
Complexity 
Care. 
The 
state 
government 
in 
Bahia 
continues 
to 
handle 
two 
thirds 
of 
the 
federal 
transfers 
for 
health. 
The 
state 
government finances 
roughly 
one quarter 
of 
public 
expenditures 
in 
health, 
nearly 
80% 
of 
that 
is 
spent 
on 
medium 
complexity 
procedures 
provided 
by 
the 
state 
government’s 
directly-owned 
network 
of 
hospitals. 
The 
state 
government 
assigns 
only 
a 
small 
proportion 
of 
its 
budget 
to 
Basic 
Care. 
The Municipalities 
finance about 
20% 
of 
total 
public 
sector 
health 
spending 
in 
Bahia 
from 
their 
own 
resources. 
Roughly 
forty 
percent 
of 
this 
is 
spent 
on 
Basic 
Care, 
with 
the 
remainder devoted 
to 
medium 
complexity 
procedures. 
There 
are 
substantial 
variations 
in 
health 
spending 
among 
individual 
municipalities, 
this 
is 
not 
surprising 
given 
that 
they 
range 
in 
population 
(from 
2,987 
to 
2.25 
million) 
and 
in 
status 
(with 
19 
certified 
for 
gestdo 
plena). 
There exist 
major 
inequalities 
in 
the geographic 
distribution 
of 
public 
health 
resources 
within 
Bahia. 
In 
per 
capita 
terms,  some 
micro-regions 
consume 
six 
to 
ten times 
more 
Complex 
Care 
than 
others. 
Inequity 
in 
the 
geographical 
distribution 
of 
spending financed 
by 
the 
state 
government 
is 
even 
greater 
than 
the 
inequity 
in 
the 
distribution 
of 
federal 
transfers. 
As 
the 
state  spends 
mostly 
on 
the 
payroll 
of 
its 
own 
hospitals, the 
localities 
where 
these 
hospitals reside 
obtain 
the 
largest 
benefits. 
The 
largest 
single 
problem 
of 
inequality 
is 
the 
concentration 
of 
health 
resources 
in 
Metropolitan 
Salvador.  Estimates 
of 
the 
benefits 
received 
by 
the 
local population 
suggest 
that 
with 
around 
18% 
of 
the 
state 
population, 
Metropolitan 
Salvador 
consumes 
30% 
of 
the 
federal  transfers 
for 
complex 
care 
and 
57% 
of 
state-financed 
health 
expenditures 
- 
this 
is 
equivalent 
to 
about 
40% 
of 
combined 
federal 
and 
state-government 
funds.3 
Despite 
the 
constitutional 
right 
to 
free 
and 
universal 
health 
care, 
households 
pay 
55% 
of 
the  cost 
of 
care. 
Households assign about 
7% 
of 
their 
total 
expenditures 
to 
health 
care. 
These 
estimates 
refer 
to 
the 
consumption 
of 
services 
by 
a 
population, 
and 
not 
to 
the 
production 
of 
services 
by 
the 
providers. 
The 
difference 
between 
these 
concepts 
is 
the 
use 
of 
referrals. 
Internationally, health 
expenditures 
are 
always 
concentrated 
in 
a 
few 
locations where 
services 
are 
concentrated 
and 
people 
from 
small 
locations 
are 
referred 
to 
the 
large cities 
to 
obtain 
complex 
health 
services. 
Referrals 
in 
Bahia 
account 
for 
a 
very small 
part 
of 
public 
health 
expenditures. 
2 
10 
Page  11
Government 
Strategy 
Following 
a 
long 
and 
participatory 
political 
process, 
the 
Government 
in 
2001 
issued 
its 
Norma 
Operacional 
de 
Assistincia 
de 
Saude 
- 
NOAS, 
which 
builds 
on 
the 
NOBs 
by 
expanding the 
use 
of 
per 
capita 
payments 
to 
cover 
a 
wider 
package 
of 
services 
and 
by 
completing 
the transfer 
of 
the 
management 
of 
federal 
funds 
to 
micro-regions. 
The 
main 
objective 
of 
NOAS 
is 
the 
promotion 
of 
greater 
equity 
and 
access 
to 
all 
levels 
of 
health 
care. 
It 
attempts 
to 
achieve 
this 
objective 
by 
breaking 
with 
the 
tradition 
of 
having 
State 
Governments 
allocate 
funds 
to 
health providers and  allocating 
them  more 
directly 
to 
populations. 
The 
municipalities 
for 
Basic 
Care 
and 
multi-municipal 
health 
micro-regions 
for 
medium 
complexity 
care 
will 
be 
“fundholders” 
for 
their  populations. 
The 
main 
features 
of 
NOAS 
are 
the 
following: 
e 
Mandates  an 
expansion 
in 
the  basic  package 
and 
in  capitation 
payments. 
It 
requires 
municipalities (who 
will 
remain 
in 
charge 
of 
Basic 
Care) 
to 
provide 
a 
wider 
array 
of 
services 
(now  called 
PAB-ampliado 
or 
PAB-A) 
to 
their  populations. 
Services 
must 
now 
include 
interventions 
for 
the 
control 
of 
tuberculosis,  Hansen’s 
disease, 
hypertension, 
diabetes, 
child 
health 
services, 
maternal 
care, 
and dental 
care, 
plus 
a 
list 
of 
specific 
diagnostic 
and 
treatment 
services. 
It 
also 
increases 
the 
share 
of 
funds 
that 
are 
distributed automatically 
on 
a 
per-capita 
basis 
to 
municipalities, 
by 
increasing the 
value 
of 
capitations 
associated 
with 
PAB-A, 
and 
by 
creating 
a 
new 
per-capita 
payment distributed 
to 
micro-regions 
to 
provide 
M1 
services. 
Creates micuo-regions 
and 
mandates 
the 
use 
of 
transparent 
systems 
for 
the 
distribution 
of 
federal 
resources 
for 
Complex 
Care. 
It 
requires 
states 
to 
divide 
their territory 
into 
multi-municipal 
health 
micro-regions  and 
to 
allocate  federal 
funds 
for 
Complex 
Care 
to 
these 
micro-regions 
using 
a 
participatory 
process 
(Programaq6o 
Pactuada  e 
Integrada 
- 
PPI 
- 
Intergovernmental 
budget 
Programming). 
Budget planning 
would 
be 
based 
on 
needs 
estimated 
using 
the 
size 
and the 
epidemiological 
status[PC2] 
of 
the 
population 
of 
each 
municipality. Micro-regions 
would 
then 
be 
entitled 
to 
manage 
their complex 
care 
budget. 
Investment 
Planning 
(developing 
a 
Plano 
Diretor 
de 
Investimento 
-PDI) 
would 
be 
based 
on 
the 
identification 
of 
supply 
gaps 
by 
micro-region 
and 
would 
assign 
new 
resources 
giving 
priority 
to 
micro-regions  that 
do not 
have the 
minimum 
required 
supply 
of 
medium 
complexity 
services. 
Establishes 
more  transparent 
systems 
to 
manage 
care 
at  the 
local 
level 
based 
on  the 
use 
of 
networks. 
It 
requires micro-regions 
to 
implement 
standardized management 
tools 
to 
improve 
the 
regulation 
of 
medium 
complexity 
care 
(Centrais 
de 
Regulaq6o). 
These management 
tools 
will 
be 
used 
to 
emphasize 
preventive 
care 
and 
to 
rationalize 
the 
use 
of 
complex 
care 
by 
strengthening 
the 
use 
of 
referrals 
from 
Basic 
Care professionals 
to 
prioritize 
access 
to 
complex 
care. 
It 
also requires 
states 
to 
implement 
transparent  management 
tools 
for 
the 
use 
of 
high 
costhigh 
complexity 
interventions. 
Makes the 
state responsible 
for  monitoring 
and 
evaluating 
the 
performance 
of 
municipalities 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
key 
health 
programs. 
States 
become responsible 
for 
ensuring compliance 
of 
municipalities 
with 
the  Basic  Care 
targets 
set 
by 
Pactos 
de 
Atenqco 
Bhsica. 
States 
also 
become 
responsible 
for 
ensuring that 
small municipalities obtain effective 
access 
to 
referrals 
in 
services 
provided 
in 
large 
municipalities 
(and 
financed 
with 
resources 
budgeted 
through 
the 
PPI). 
e 
e 
3. 
Sector 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
by 
the 
project 
and 
strategic 
choices: 
Basic 
Care 
issues 
Bahia 
achieved 
very  significant 
improvements 
in 
health 
outcomes 
during 
the 
1990s, 
with 
the 
infant 
mortality 
rate 
improving 
by 
20% 
from 
1992 
to 
1998. 
However, 
this 
progress 
in 
the 
reduction 
of 
the 
infant mortality 
rate 
was 
slower 
in 
Bahia 
than 
for 
Brazil 
as 
a 
whole (which 
had 
an improvement 
of 
26% 
in 
the 
same 
period). 
The most 
basic 
indicators 
remain 
at 
poor 
levels 
in 
Bahia. 
For 
this 
reason, 
it 
needs 
to 
continue 
strengthening 
primary 
care 
for 
the 
population. 
Two 
innovations 
- 
closely 
linked 
to 
the 
11 
Page  12
introduction 
of 
PAB-- 
are 
improving 
Basic 
Care 
in 
Brazil 
and 
in 
Bahia: 
the 
implementation 
of 
new 
mechanisms 
to 
introduce 
greater 
accountability 
in 
the 
delivery 
of 
primary 
care 
(the 
Pacto 
de 
AtenGZo 
Basica 
or 
Basic 
Health 
Agreement), 
and 
the 
introduction 
of 
new 
“models” 
for 
the 
delivery 
of 
Basic 
Care. 
The 
Pacto 
de 
Atenqio 
Bcisica 
is 
the 
main 
instrument 
of 
accountability 
to 
assess 
how 
municipalities 
are 
performing 
their 
duty 
of 
providing 
Basic 
Care 
to 
their 
populations. 
There 
is 
a 
Pacto 
for 
each 
municipality, 
consisting 
of 
municipal 
targets 
for 
a 
small 
number 
of 
performance indicators and 
listing 
commitments 
to 
support the 
municipality 
by 
the 
state 
and federal governments. 
The 
selection 
of 
the 
indicators 
is 
the 
result 
of 
a 
complex 
process 
that 
identifies 
the 
key 
(intermediate) 
interventions 
needed 
to reach 
the 
main 
health 
priorities 
of 
the nation, 
which 
include 
reductions 
in 
infant 
mortality, 
in 
matemal 
mortality 
and 
in 
the 
incidence 
of 
transmissible 
di~ease.~ 
In 
2000, 
412 
municipalities 
signed 
Pactos. 
An 
evaluation 
of 
the experience 
concluded that 
there was 
a 
high 
degree 
of 
success 
for 
the 
surveillance indicators 
(95% 
of 
municipalities 
reached 
their 
targets), 
but 
there 
were 
low 
degrees 
of 
success 
for 
the 
indicators measuring 
outcomes 
and 
those 
measuring 
coverage 
of 
priority 
health 
programs (fewer than 
40% 
of 
municipalities 
reached 
their 
targets 
in 
these categories). 
When 
all 
indicators 
are 
considered 
together, 
half 
of 
the 
municipalities 
could 
not 
reach even 
50% 
of 
their 
targets. 
By 
law, 
starting 
in 
2001 municipalities 
that 
either 
refuse 
to 
sign 
a 
Pacto 
or 
that 
sign 
a 
Pacto 
and 
fail 
to 
reach 
their 
targets 
may 
be 
penalized 
with 
a 
loss 
in 
their 
eligibility 
to 
receive 
PAB 
transfers, 
however, 
and 
given 
the 
extended 
failure 
on 
reaching 
targets, 
this 
penalty 
has 
been waived. 
Efforts 
are 
being 
made 
all 
over 
Brazil 
to improve 
the 
way 
the 
Pactos 
work, 
by 
improving 
the 
process 
of 
negotiating 
and 
planning 
targets 
and 
by 
more effectively 
linking 
targets 
with 
the 
use 
of 
available 
resources. 
The 
project 
would 
contribute 
in 
fine-tuning 
the 
Pactos 
in 
Bahia. 
For 
clinical 
basic 
services, 
there 
are 
two  new 
models 
in 
operation. 
The 
Family  Health 
Program 
(Programa 
de 
Satide 
da 
Familia 
-- 
PSF) 
was  launched 
in 
1994, 
building 
on 
the 
strengths 
of 
its 
1991 
predecessor, 
the 
Programa 
de 
AtenqZo 
Comunitaria 
-PAC. 
The 
program 
seeks 
to 
develop 
high-quality 
primary 
care services 
that 
serve 
vulnerable populations 
in 
a 
proactive 
manner; 
eventually 
these 
services 
are 
expected 
to 
serve 
as 
an 
entry 
point 
to 
higher-level 
services. 
PAC 
introduced 
the 
use 
of 
paid 
health 
agents 
who 
reach out 
to 
the 
community 
in 
an 
organized fashion 
- 
for 
example, 
by 
creating 
a 
roster 
of 
the 
full 
beneficiary 
population 
in 
a 
given 
zone; 
identifying 
pregnant 
women, 
young 
children, 
tuberculosis 
patients, 
and 
others 
in 
need 
of 
frequent 
attention  and 
follow-up;  and 
providing 
home-based 
care 
or 
actively 
encouraging  patients 
to 
come 
to 
health  facilities 
for 
necessary  services. 
PSF 
organized 
community 
agents 
into 
teams, 
which 
typically 
include 
a 
general 
practice 
or 
family 
health 
physician, 
a 
nurse, 
one 
or 
two 
auxiliary 
nurses, and, 
more 
recently, 
a 
part-time 
dentist 
and/or 
dental technician. 
Each 
team 
is 
assigned the 
responsibility 
of 
providing 
care 
to 
a 
pre-defined 
set 
of 
families. 
Nationally, 
the 
PSF 
covers 
nearly one-fourth 
of 
the 
Brazilian 
population. 
In 
Bahia 
it 
covers 
11% 
of 
the 
population. 
Progress 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
program 
differs 
by 
municipality 
size 
and 
by 
region. 
Most 
PSF 
teams are 
in 
small municipalities. However, 
because 
there 
are 
so 
many 
small 
municipalities, 
only 
20% 
of 
them 
have 
joined 
the 
PSF 
program. 
Ten 
of 
the 
12 
large 
municipalities 
have inaugurated 
the 
program, 
but 
nine 
of 
them 
have 
just 
one 
or 
two 
PSF 
teams 
and 
consequently 
very 
low 
coverage 
rates. 
Most 
PSF 
teams  are 
located 
in 
the 
better-developed 
and 
more 
accessible 
coastal regions, 
with 
significantly fewer 
teams 
in 
municipalities located 
in 
the 
poorer 
and 
less accessible 
interior. 
Expansion 
of 
PSF 
will 
require  overcoming 
difficult 
challenges. 
In 
rural 
areas 
the 
main 
problem 
is 
attracting 
and 
retaining 
physicians and 
other professionals. 
PSF 
professionals 
in 
the 
country 
are 
hired 
under temporary 
contracts 
and 
are 
highly 
paid 
to 
attract 
them 
to 
difficult 
locations: they receive 
several 
times 
the 
take-home 
pay 
of 
comparable 
professionals 
employed 
as 
civil 
servants. 
Mayors 
from 
small 
municipalities 
claim 
that 
at 
these rates, 
the cost 
of 
PSF 
teams 
is 
several 
times 
higher 
than the 
subsidy 
they 
These 
priorities 
are 
consistent 
with 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals 
(MDGs). 
4 
12 
Page  13
receive 
from 
the 
Federal Government and that they 
cannot 
afford 
to 
expand the program. 
The 
expansion 
of 
PSF 
in 
urban 
locations 
faces 
a 
different 
set 
of 
challenges. 
While 
there 
exist  pockets 
of 
poor 
populations that 
do not 
have 
access 
to 
health 
care, 
in 
urban 
areas 
there 
already 
exist 
significant 
resources 
in 
the 
form 
of 
traditional 
primary 
care 
clinics and hospital 
services. 
These 
services 
suffer 
from 
significant 
inefficiencies 
and 
the 
main 
objective 
of 
expanding 
PSF 
in 
urban 
locations 
is 
to 
introduce 
reforms 
that 
would 
lead 
to 
improvements 
in 
the 
health 
status 
of 
the 
poor 
and 
to 
a 
more 
efficient 
use 
of 
the 
existing 
resources. 
The 
project 
would 
contribute 
(with 
sub-projects 
and 
by 
supporting 
a 
shift 
in 
state-government 
spending) 
to 
expand PSF where 
feasible. 
The 
feasibility 
of 
health 
reaching the desired universal 
coverage 
of 
the 
population 
with 
PSF 
will 
require significant 
increases 
in 
financing 
for 
the 
program 
and 
the 
release 
of 
health 
professionals 
from 
tertiary 
care. 
The 
Bank, 
through 
the 
HD 
adjustment 
loan 
the 
Family 
Health 
Expansion Program 
is 
supporting the Government to 
achieve 
these 
objectives. 
Internationally, 
a 
major 
weakness 
of 
Basic 
Care 
is 
the 
frequent 
lack 
of 
basic 
pharmaceuticals 
at the 
local 
level. 
In 
Bahia 
availability 
has 
improved 
substantially 
with 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Basic 
Pharmaceuticals 
program 
(Assistincia 
Farmaceutica 
Bcisica), 
which 
consists 
of 
a 
[PC3]transfer 
of 
R$2 
per 
person per 
year 
to 
municipalities 
designed 
to 
provide 
a 
basic 
set 
of 
drugs 
for 
the 
population. 
The 
program 
is 
financed 
jointly 
by 
the 
federal 
government, 
which 
contributes 
R$1, 
the 
state (R$0.5), 
and 
the 
municipality 
(R$0.5). 
Municipalities 
can participate 
in 
two 
ways: 
receiving 
the 
funding 
directly 
from 
the 
federal government and 
making 
their 
own 
purchases 
or 
by 
using 
SESAB 
as 
a 
purchasing 
agent. 
While 
this 
program 
has 
improved 
availability 
of 
pharmaceuticals,  there 
remains 
some 
duplication 
between 
several 
sources 
of 
drugs, 
inefficiency 
in 
the 
purchasing 
process 
and 
other constraints 
in 
the 
distribution 
system. 
There 
is 
a 
trade-off 
between 
price 
and 
opportunity 
in 
the 
purchases. 
SESAB 
purchases 
large 
quantities and 
obtains 
lower 
prices than 
individual 
municipalities. 
However, 
some 
municipalities 
opted 
for 
managing 
the 
purchase 
directly 
to  avoid 
delays. 
In 
2002, 
the 
state 
of 
Bahia 
passed 
legislation 
allowing 
the 
use 
of 
E-procurement 
for 
these purchases, 
this 
new 
procedure 
may 
overcome the 
trade-off 
between 
price 
and opportunity. 
Under 
the 
proposed 
system, 
estimates 
of 
need 
will 
be 
made 
locally 
and 
consolidated centrally. 
A 
central 
authority 
will 
then 
invite 
bids 
for 
the 
estimated 
total 
volume 
of 
needs 
and 
suppliers 
will 
compete 
with 
proposals 
that 
include 
price 
and 
cost 
of 
delivery 
to different 
locations. 
The 
winning 
proposals 
will 
be 
valid 
for 
a 
set 
period 
of 
time 
and 
their 
conditions 
will 
be 
posted 
on 
the 
Internet. 
Local 
users 
can then 
make 
the purchase 
directly 
from 
the 
suppliers at 
the 
agreed 
price. 
The 
project 
will 
support  the  basic 
pharmacy 
program 
by 
implementing 
the 
new 
planning 
and 
purchasing 
technology 
and 
by 
solving 
other 
bottlenecks 
in 
the distribution 
chain. 
Capacity Building 
A 
significant 
challenge 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
NOAS 
is 
that 
it 
requires 
substantial 
capacity 
of 
implementation 
at 
the 
level 
of 
the 
state, 
the 
micro-regions 
and 
the municipalities. 
At 
the 
level 
of 
the 
state, 
it 
requires 
a 
redefinition 
of 
the 
role 
of 
the 
state 
government--from 
manager 
of 
federal 
funds 
and provider 
of 
services 
through 
state 
hospitals 
to 
policy 
maker and 
overseer 
of 
quality. 
In 
its 
new 
role, 
it 
will 
need 
a 
substantial 
improvement 
in 
the 
quality 
of 
data 
it 
possesses--starting 
with 
data 
on 
the 
health 
of 
the 
population, 
which 
is 
at 
present 
of 
dubious 
quality 
(the 
Federal  Government 
estimates 
that 
Bahia 
underestimates 
infant mortality 
by 
66%), 
in 
its 
capacity 
of 
analyzing 
these 
data 
and 
in 
its 
capacity 
for 
policy 
development. 
It 
will 
also 
require 
that 
SESAB 
develops 
new 
models 
of 
regulation, 
moving 
away 
from 
the 
direct 
purchase 
of 
services--which 
for 
basic 
and 
medium 
complexity 
will 
be 
decentralized 
to 
municipalities and 
micro-regions--to 
a 
position 
of 
performance-target setting 
and 
monitoring 
of 
quality 
for 
basic 
and 
medium 
complexity 
care. 
It 
also 
requires that clear and 
transparent 
rules 
are 
developed 
for 
the 
management 
and 
resource-allocation 
in 
high 
complexity 
care. 
A 
second 
significant 
challenge 
is 
the development 
of 
a 
capacity 
for 
management 
in 
the 
municipality 
of 
Salvador. 
As 
described 
above, 
a 
large 
proportion 
of 
state 
services are 
provided 
in 
Metropolitan 
Salvador. 
Today, 
these 
services 
are 
provided 
in 
public 
hospitals 
run 
directly 
by 
SESAB 
and 
by 
private 
hospitals 
selling 
services 
to 
SESAB. 
Salvador 
has 
not had 
the 
need 
to 
develop 
its 
capacity 
to 
manage 
health 
care 
13 
Page  14
and 
has 
not 
achieved the 
status 
of 
Gestiio 
Plena--this 
forces 
SESAB 
to 
concentrate 
much 
of 
its 
energy 
on 
the 
direct 
provision 
of 
services 
and 
delays 
the  development 
of 
its 
capacity 
for  policy 
making 
and 
regulation. 
The  next 
section 
describes 
key 
challenges 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
micro-regions 
and 
opportunities 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
new 
forms 
of 
regulation. 
Management 
and Governance of 
Micro-regions 
NOAS 
attempts 
to 
correct 
inefficiencies 
and inequities 
by 
decentralizing 
management 
responsibilities 
currently 
held 
by 
SESAB 
to 
municipalities 
and micro-regions. 
Municipalities 
and micro-regions 
would 
be 
provided 
with 
management tools. 
Specifically, 
NOAS 
introduces 
Regulation 
Centers 
(Centrais 
de 
Regulaqiio 
ou 
de 
MarcaqGo), 
selective contracting, 
and 
systems 
of 
audit and 
review. 
A 
Regulation 
Center 
is 
an 
office 
that 
assigns 
a 
patient 
to 
a 
provider 
for 
a 
particular 
procedure. 
A 
representative 
of 
the patient’s 
primary 
health 
team 
makes 
a 
referral 
request 
for 
these 
specialized 
services. 
The 
Regulation 
Center 
then 
assigns 
the 
patient 
a 
specific 
date 
and 
time 
with 
the 
requested 
provider, 
or 
redirects the 
patient 
to 
another 
available 
provider 
if 
necessary. 
In 
the 
area 
of 
planning, 
this 
system 
offers 
the 
capacity 
to 
manage 
care 
by 
prioritizing 
referrals 
on 
the basis 
of 
need 
based 
on 
a 
review 
for 
appropriateness 
and 
medical 
necessity. 
The 
data 
derived 
from 
Regulation 
Centers 
also 
allow 
planning 
to 
reduce 
waiting 
times 
for 
patients. 
In 
the 
area 
of 
regulation, 
the 
Centers 
also have 
great 
advantages. 
When 
all 
appointments 
are 
made 
by 
the 
Center, 
then 
the Center 
will 
only 
reimburse 
for 
services 
previously 
approved 
by 
it, 
reducing the 
scope 
for 
fraud. 
Also, 
the 
regulation 
centers 
can 
establish 
significant 
improvements 
in 
controls 
and 
audits. 
In 
some 
municipalities 
with 
Gestiio Plena, 
patients 
scheduled 
for 
elective surgery receive 
a 
visit 
in 
their 
home to 
confirm 
that  the 
condition 
matches  the  diagnosis 
and 
that 
the  patient 
is 
a 
resident 
of 
the 
municipality. 
Site 
visits 
are 
scheduled 
to 
high-volume  providers  and 
clinical 
laboratories. 
As 
a 
complement 
to 
the 
regulation 
centers, 
municipalities 
in 
GestGo 
Plena 
are 
empowered 
to 
strengthen the 
terms 
of 
their 
provider 
contracts. 
Only 
a 
few 
municipalities have 
taken 
this 
step, 
but 
there 
are 
already 
examples 
where 
strategies 
such 
as 
selective 
contracting 
have 
been 
used 
successfully 
to improve 
provider 
performance and 
to 
control 
price 
and 
supply. 
While 
NOAS 
is 
very 
detailed 
in 
some 
areas, 
the  governance 
system 
for 
the 
micro-regions 
remains 
undefined. 
This 
is 
a 
significant vacuum 
as 
there 
needs 
to 
be 
an institutional 
arrangement 
that provides 
incentives 
for 
municipalities 
to 
pool 
resources 
to 
obtain 
better 
services 
for 
their 
population 
giving-up 
some 
of 
the 
political 
clout 
and 
patronage 
that come 
from 
directly 
managing 
resources. 
Three models 
are 
being 
tested 
in 
different 
parts 
of 
Brazil. 
In 
Ceara, 
the 
micro-regions 
are 
managed 
mainly 
by 
the 
regional 
outposts 
of 
the 
state 
health 
secretariat 
with 
varying 
degrees 
of 
participation 
by 
municipalities. 
In 
some 
areas 
of 
the country, 
micro-regions 
are 
formed 
by 
consortia 
of 
municipalities 
that 
create 
an 
ad 
hoc 
agency 
for 
managing 
health 
care 
with 
no 
participation 
of 
the 
state. 
In 
other 
states 
(including 
Bahia) 
the 
prevailing 
model 
is 
to 
allow 
a 
municipality 
in 
Gestiio 
Plena 
(“municipio-polo”) 
to 
manage 
part 
of 
the budget 
of 
neighboring 
smaller 
municipalities 
(“satellites”) 
with 
varying 
participation 
of 
the satellites 
and 
of 
the 
state 
in 
the 
daily 
management 
of 
resources 
or 
in 
oversight. 
The 
project 
will 
support 
the 
test 
and 
adaptation 
of 
governance structures 
in 
micro-regions. 
4. 
Program 
description 
and 
performance 
triggers for 
subsequent 
loans: 
The Bahia 
Health 
System 
Reform 
Project 
consists 
of 
complementary 
strategies 
designed 
to  improve 
health 
outcomes 
and 
to 
increase 
access, 
reduce 
inequalities 
and 
raise 
efficiency 
of 
health 
care. 
Through 
APL 
1, 
the 
Government 
of 
Bahia 
will: 
(i) 
improve 
the 
operation 
of 
Performance 
Agreements 
for 
Basic 
Care 
with 
municipalities and 
strengthen 
the 
system 
of 
municipal 
incentives 
for 
Basic 
Care; 
(ii) 
Test 
Governance 
and 
management structures 
in 
five 
micro-regions and initiate 
the 
implementation 
of 
a 
high 
complexity 
Regulation 
Center; 
(iii) 
strengthen 
the capacity 
of 
the 
municipality 
of 
Salvador, 
preparing 
it 
to 
assume 
Gestiio Plena; 
and 
(iv) 
begin to 
shift 
the focus 
of 
SESAB 
away 
from 
the 
provision 
of 
services 
and 
14 
Page  15
begin 
to 
strengthen 
its 
capacity 
for policy 
implementation and 
regulation, 
and 
(v) 
obtain 
data 
and 
analysis 
on 
the 
health 
needs 
of 
excluded populations. 
Through 
APL2, 
the 
Government 
will: 
(i) 
consolidate 
the 
use 
of 
Performance 
Agreements-expanding 
their 
use 
beyond 
Basic 
Care; 
(ii) 
Expand 
the 
implementation 
of 
micro-regions  and 
consolidate  the 
regulation 
of 
high 
complexity 
care; 
(iii) 
support 
the 
implementation 
of 
Gestio 
Plena 
in 
Salvador; 
and 
(iv) 
further reduce 
SESAB’s 
participation 
in 
the 
provision 
of 
services 
and consolidate 
its 
role 
as 
a 
regulator 
of 
health 
care, 
and 
(v) 
implement 
specific 
interventions 
to 
better 
serve 
excluded populations. 
Performance triggers 
for 
the 
initiation 
of 
APL2 
are: 
The 
success 
rate 
of 
the 
Basic 
Care 
Performance Agreements 
(Pactos) 
increased 
from 
0% 
in 
2001 
to 
50% 
in 
2007 
(Success 
rate 
is 
defined 
as 
the 
percent 
of 
the 
state 
municipalities 
that  have 
reached 
at least 
8 
of 
their 
targets 
for 
the 
10 
Indicators 
agreed 
for 
the project); 
The 
use 
of 
federal 
and 
state 
funds 
for 
health 
care 
by 
the 
population 
of 
Salvador 
has 
been reduced 
from 
40% 
to 
35% 
of 
the 
total for 
the 
state 
of 
Bahia; 
Regulation 
systems 
implemented 
in 
at least 
five 
micro-regions 
(this 
will 
include: 
(i) 
Management 
of 
at  least 
10% 
of 
the 
budget 
of 
the 
participating municipalities 
by 
a 
unit 
accountable 
to 
all 
municipalities participating 
in 
the 
micro-region; 
(ii) 
a 
system 
of 
audits 
for 
services 
performed 
implemented; 
(iii) 
a 
system 
of 
quality 
accreditation 
implemented); 
A 
Regulation 
Center 
for 
High 
Complexity 
Interventions 
is 
in 
operation 
(with 
public information 
about 
waiting 
lists 
and 
the 
use 
of 
public 
funds); 
The 
Municipality 
of 
Salvador 
has 
received 
support 
from 
the 
Govemment 
of 
Bahia 
to 
become 
accredited 
for 
Gestio 
Plena 
(according

the 
action

in 
files); 
Small Hospitals 
(under 
50 
beds) 
directly

by 
SESAB 
reduced 
to 
no 
more 
than 
10; 
C. 
Program 
and 
Project 
Description 
Summary

Implementation 
of 
health 
micro-repions 
in 
Bahia. 
fUS$18.3 
million, 
including 
continpencies) 
A. 
Strengthening 
management 
and governance 
of 
micro-regions. 
To 
strengthen the 
role 
of 
the 
micro- 
region 
as 
a 
purchaser 
of 
health 
care 
services, 
the 
project 
will 
support: 
(i) 
technical 
assistance 
for 
the 
deployment 
of 
performance 
based contracts 
between 
the 
micro-regions and 
their 
local 
providers, 
(ii) 
TA, 
works 
and 
equipment 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
Regulation 
Centers; 
and 
(iii) 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
for 
the 
adaptation 
of 
SUS 
card technology 
to 
the 
local 
conditions  and 
the 
implementation 
of 
applications that 
address 
the 
needs 
of 
microregional 
management. 
To 
strengthen the 
capacity 
of 
micro- 
regions 
to 
conduct 
clinical, 
environmental  and 
financial 
audits 
of 
providers 
and 
to 
initiate 
a process 
of 
accreditation, 
the 
project 
will 
support: 
(i) 
turn-key 
contracts 
to 
develop 
auditing 
and  accreditation 
systems; 
(ii) 
technical 
assistance 
for 
the 
preparation 
of 
Medical 
Waste 
Plans 
in 
health 
care 
facilities and 
implementation 
of 
existing 
local 
environmental 
assessment 
and licensing 
of 
health 
centers; 
and 
(iii) 
training 
to 
teach audits 
and 
accreditation 
techniques. 
Micro-regions 
must 
develop 
governance 
systems 
that balance the 
priorities, 
needs 
and capacities 
of 
large 
municipalities, 
their 
small 
surrounding 
municipalities and the 
state. 
No 
firm 
blueprint 
exists 
for 
this 
system 
of 
governance, 
and 
they 
will 
need 
to 
evolve 
based 
on 
trial 
and 
error. 
The 
systems 
will 
be defined 
during 
implementation. 
The 
project 
will 
support 
this 
process 
by 
financing: 
(i) 
the 
initial 
costs 
of 
implementing 
local 
governing 
bodies, 
(ii) 
the 
implementation 
of 
micro-regional planning and 
budgeting 
15 
Page  16
tools, 
(iii) 
meetings 
and 
seminars 
for 
municipal 
health 
secretaries, 
and 
field 
trips 
for 
members 
of 
the 
goveming 
body 
to 
visit 
other 
states 
that 
have 
successfully 
implemented micro-regions, 
(iv) 
monitoring 
and evaluation activities.

polo

 condition^:^

the 
status 
of 
implementation 
of 
municipal 
health 
management 
programs  mandated 
by 
the  federal 
and 
state 
governments, 
a 
local 
environmental 
health 
plan,  estimated 
costs 
of 
the 
sub-project,  a 
sustainability 
proposal 
including 
the 
use 
of 
municipal 
and 
state 
funds 
that 
will 
be 
phased-in 
as 
project 
funds 
face  out,  a 
procurement 
plan 
for 
sub-project 
implementation and 
a 
disbursement 
plan. 
In 
order 
to 
overcome  capacity 
constraints, 
the 
project 
will 
finance technical 
assistance 
to 
interested 
municipalities, 
and 
the 
regional 
outposts 
of 
SESAB 
will 
receive 
special 
training  to 
support 
municipalities 
in 
the 
preparation 
of 
the 
Plans. 
Also, 
the 
participating 
municipalities 
will 
be 
clustered 
together 
in 
a 
few 
micro-regions 
during 
the 
initial 
years 
of 
implementation 
favoring 
cross-leaming 
and 
making 
it 
easier 
to provide them 
with 
technical 
assistance. 
The 
Basic 
Care 
Expansion 
Plans 
will 
form 
the 
basis 
for 
subproject 
Implementation 
Agreements 
between  the 
municipalities  and 
the 
State, 
which 
will 
set 
forth 
the 
respective 
obligations 
regarding 
subproject 
implementation. 
If 
in 
any 
year the 
municipality 
fails 
to 
achieve at least 
6 
of 
the 
10 
targets, 
SESAB, 
in 
coordination 
with 
the Bank, 
may 
decide 
to 
suspend disbursements. 
There 
will 
be 
minimum 
criteria 
for 
eligibility 
and 
a 
system 
of 
points for 
the 
final 
selection 
of 
municipalities. 
Eligibility 
of 
subprojects, 
of 
implementing 
municipalities  and 
of 
expenditures 
to 
be 
financed 
under 
the 
subprojects 
as 
well 
as 
implementation 
arrangements 
(procurement, 
disbursement, 
selection, 
approval, 
environmental 
requirements, 
monitoring 
and 
supervision) 
will 
be 
detailed 
in 
an 
operational  manual. 
Subprojects 
estimated 
to 
cost 
US$500,000 
equivalent 
or 
more 
will 
be 
subject 
to 
prior 
review 
by 
the 
Bank 
before the 
Term0 
de 
Compromisso 
is 
signed.

3. 
Benefits and 
target 
population: 
The program 
is 
designed 
to improve 
health 
outcomes 
of 
the 
population 
of 
Bahia 
by 
expanding 
access 
and 
quality 
of 
care 
and 
to 
improve 
efficiency 
in 
the 
use 
of 
public 
resources. 
Most 
of 
the 
improvement 
in 
Basic 
health 
outcomes 
is 
expected 
from 
the expansion 
of 
Basic 
Care 
that 
will 
result 
from 
a 
combination 
of 
improved 
policies 
for 
Basic 
Care 
and 
investments 
financed 
by 
municipal 
subprojects. 
Basic 
Care 
policies 
will 
be 
improved 
with 
project 
support 
for policy 
development, 
for 
strengthening 
the Basic pharmacy 
program 
and 
for 
information, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation. 
This 
support 
will 
increase 
the 
impact 
of 
existing 
resources and 
may 
also 
result 
in 
redirecting additional 
municipal 
and 
state-government 
resources 
to 
Basic 
Care 
and 
would 
benefit 
the 
entire 
population 
of 
Bahia. 
Municipal 
sub-projects 
are 
expected 
to 
benefit 
the 
population 
of 
around 
100 
municipalities 
during 
the 
two 
phases 
of 
the 
APL 
covering 
around 
5 
million 
people 
(around 
40 
municipalities  covering 
around 
2 
million 
people 
in 
APL1). 
Targets 
for 
improvements 
in 
outcomes 
and 
in 
intermediate 
programs 
are 
described 
in 
Annex 
1. 
Increased 
efficiency 
in 
the 
use 
of 
public 
resources 
will 
result 
from 
improved 
management 
of 
the 
health 
system. 
These 
improvements 
will 
occur 
at: 
(i) 
the 
municipal level 
as 
a 
result 
of 
strengthened 
Basic Care 
agreements 
and 
(in 
participating 
municipalities) 
as 
a 
result 
of 
municipal 
subprojects; 
(ii) 
the 
micro-regional 
level 
as 
a 
result 
of 
implementing 
new 
forms 
of 
governance and management 
of 
systems 
and 
as 
a 
result 
of 
greater 
transparency 
from 
the 
implementation 
of 
audits; 
and 
(iii) 
the 
state 
level 
as 
a 
result 
of 
improved 
management 
of 
high 
complexity 
care 
and 
of 
freeing 
SESAB 
from 
the 
responsibility 
of 
managing the 
demanding 
health 
care 
system 
of 
Metropolitan 
Salvador. 
4. 
Institutional 
and 
implementation 
arrangements: 
The Bahia 
Health 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
implemented over 
a 
4 
year 
period, 
from 
July 
2003 
through 
June 
2007. 
The 
program 
will 
be 
subject 
to 
regular 
evaluations 
every 
six 
months, 
with 
yearly 
evaluations 
that 
will 
include an 
assessment 
of 
progress 
towards 
all 
performance 
triggers. 
SESAB 
(Secretaria 
de 
Sazide 
da Bahia) 
will 
be 
the 
principal 
executing 
agency 
for 
the 
program 
through 
a 
project coordination 
unit 
(PCU), 
created 
in 
2002. 
Its 
responsibilities 
will 
include: 
(i) 
financial 
oversight 
of 
the  program; 
(ii) 
coordination 
with 
line 
agencies 
and 
departments, 
regional  offices 
and  external 
agencies 
responsible 
for program 
activities; 
(iii) 
coordination 
with 
municipal 
governments 
participating 
in 
the program; 
and 
(iv) 
overall 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation activities. 
Responsibility 
for 
program 
activities 
will 
be 
coordinated 
by 
the 
PCU 
and  implemented 
by 
SESAB 
line 
agencies 
and 
by 
participating 
municipalities. 
All 
participating municipalities 
will 
sign 
performance 
agreements 
with 
SESAB. 
Procurement 
and financial 
management 
arrangements  are 
described 
in 
Annex 
6. 
Procurement 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
by: 
(i) 
SESAB’s 
specialized procurement 
department 
and 
its 
regional 
offices; 
(ii) 
the 
State 
Secretariat 
of 
Administration 
(for 
vehicles  and 
civil 
works); 
and 
(iii) 
participating municipalities (for 
subprojects). 
SESAB 
will 
strengthen 
the procurement 
and financial 
management 
capacity 
of 
the 
PCU 
by 
not 
later than 
three 
months 
after 
effectiveness. 
A 
procurement 
plan for 
the 
first 
year has 
been prepared 
and 
detailed 
arrangements 
will 
be 
described 
in 
the 
operational 
manual 
of 
the 
project.  Financial 
management 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
the 
financial 
team 
of 
the 
PCU 
according 
to 
the 
detail 
described 
in 
annex 
6. 
This 
team 
will 
also 
carry 
out 
risk 
assessments 
of 
the 
participating municipalities. 
An 
integrated 
information 
system 
is 
being 
developed 
for  SESAB 
based 
on 
the 
highly 
rated 
system 
in 
use 
in 
the 
Secretariat 
of 
Education 
of 
Bahia. 
The 
Tribunal 
de 
Contas 
do 
Estado 
da Bahia 
(TCEBA) 
will 
carry 
out 
the annual 
financial 
audits 
of 
the 
project 
accounts. 
The 
project 
will 
report 
in 
the 
format 
of 
Financial 
Management 
Reports 
on 
a 
quarterly 
basis.

de

SUCAB

D. 
Project 
Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
Lending 
instrument: 
APL 
versus 
SIL. 
The 
APL 
was chosen 
given: 
(i) 
the 
long 
term 
horizon 
needed 
for 
meeting 
the 
project’s  development 
objectives; 
(ii) 
the 
existence 
of 
a 
sound 
sector 
reform 
program 
developed 
by 
the 
state 
and 
the 
federal govemments; 
(iii) 
the 
need 
for 
phasing 
the 
reform 
program 
because 
of 
the 
level 
of 
complexity 
of 
some 
project 
activities, 
the 
relatively 
weak 
institutional 
capacity 
of 
the 
implementing 
agencies, 
and 
the need 
for 
testing 
new 
interventions. 
The 
APL 
will 
facilitate 
the 
“leam 
as 
you 
go” 
approach 
when implementing 
some 
of 
the 
project 
activities. 
Lending directly 
to 
the state 
versus 
lending 
to 
the 
federal 
government. 
All 
the 
previous 
13 
World Bank 
loans 
for 
health 
in 
Brazil 
have been 
with 
the Federal 
Govemment. 
The 
proposed 
project 
will 
be 
the 
first 
health 
operation 
with 
the 
state 
level. 
Other 
sectors, 
such 
as 
education, 
have 
already 
followed 
this 
strategy 
and 
with 
good 
results. 
The 
rationale 
is 
the 
following: 
(i) 
decision 
making, 
management 
and financing 
of 
health 
services 
haws 
been decentralized 
to 
the 
state 
and 
municipal 
levels; 
(ii) 
the 
size 
of 
the 
country and 
its 
Fgreat 
regional diversity 
requires 
different 
approaches 
to solving 
regional 
and 
local 
health 
problems; 
I 
(iii) 
it 
facilitates investments 
to 
the poorest regions 
of 
the 
country. 
Investing 
only 
in primary 
care 
versus 
investing 
in 
integrated 
services. 
Investing 
only 
in 
primary 
care 
is 
not 
an 
effective 
option 
since 
Bahia’s 
epidemiological 
profile 
requires 
services 
of 
higher 
levels 
of 
complexity. 
Investment 
in 
primary 
care 
without 
effective 
support 
of 
referral 
levels 
runs 
the 
risk 
of 
providing 
ineffective 
treatment 
of 
important 
pathologies 
and eventually 
damages 
the 
prestige 
of 
the 
primary 
care 
network.

Lessons 
learned 
and 
reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
The

preparation team 
carried out

on 
lessons 
learned 
from 
other 
projects 
to 
improve project 
design 
and implementation 
strategies. 
Various 
sources 
of 
information 
were consulted 
including 
the 
World 
Bank’s 
operational and evaluation 
work. 
The 
team 
also 
reviewed 
technical 
and 
evaluation 
work 
done 
by 
other development 
agencies 
and 
looked 
at 
relevant 
experiences 
produced 
in 
the 
Brazilian 
context. 
Lessons 
learned 
from 
the 
Bank’s 
operational and 
evaluation 
work,

extracted

i)

from

Brazilian

Services 
(98),

and

Control 
(98),

(97),

(97);

Mid-tem-review 
of

project 
(99);

reports: 
Investing 
in

Effectiveness 
in

Health, 
Nutrition, 
and Population

(99),

Impact Evaluation 
Report 
(98). 
The 
main 
lessons 
from

are

In

into

Lessons 
learned

Summary Project Analysis 
(Detailed 
assessments 
are 
in 
the 
project 
file, 
see 
Annex 
8) 
1. 
Economic 
(see 
Annex 
4): 
Cost 
benefit NPV=US$394 
million; 
ERR 
= 
85 
% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
This 
section 
presents 
the 
summary 
results 
of 
the 
cost-benefit  analysis 
of 
annex  4. 
The 
project 
will 
potentially 
save 
over 
16,000 
lives 
through 
improvements 
in 
the 
health 
care 
system 
and 
reductions 
in 
disability 
and 
mortality 
will 
contribute over 
400,000 
productive 
life 
years 
(DALYs) 
to 
the economy. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
project 
costs 
of 
$168 
million, 
the 
analysis 
includes  investment 
costs 
and  recurrent 
expenditures 
related 
to 
improving 
access 
to  primary 
health 
care  services, 
strengthening  emergency 
services, 
and restructuring 
the 
hospital network. 
In 
summary, 
the 
project 
would yield 
a 
present 
value 
of 
net 
benefits, 
after investment and recurrent 
costs, 
of 
over $138.5 
million 
and 
produce 
an 
intemal 
rate 
of 
return 
(IRR) 
above 
84% 
over 
ten 
years. 
The Bahia 
health 
sector 
reform 
project 
will 
produce 
substantial 
savings 
for 
the 
sector, 
primarily 
through 
reductions 
in 
hospitalization 
costs. 
The 
cost-benefit 
analysis, 
gives the 
project 
a 
BenefiKost 
Index 
of 
3.84. 
2. 
Financial 
(see 
Annex 
4 
and 
Annex 
5): 
Projected recurrent 
expenditures 
generated 
by 
the 
project 
and 
the 
estimates 
of 
future 
health 
care 
spending 
show 
that  the 
project 
would 
be  financially 
sustainable. 
Based 
on 
the  current  projections 
for 
health 
expenditures, 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project 
will 
cost 
less 
than 
2% 
of 
the 
public 
expenditure 
on 
health 
care 
services 
in 
Bahia 
starting 
from 
the 
seventh year 
of 
the 
project.  Recurrent 
costs 
attributable 
to 
recurrent 
costs 
from 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project 
represent 
additional 
costs 
of 
approximately 
5% 
above 
public 
health 
expenditures 
during 
the 
first 
six 
years 
of 
the 
project. 
These 
costs 
range 
fi-om 
8% 
above 
base 
expenditures 
in 
the 
first 
year 
to 
a 
minimum 
of 
3% 
in 
the 
sixth 
year. 
In 
the 
following 
years, 
additional 
costs due 
to 
the 
project 
represent 
only 
an 
average 
of 
1% 
increase 
over 
normal 
expenditures. 
3. 
Technical: 
The 
project 
will 
have 
a 
phased approach 
to 
implementing 
technical 
solutions, 
which 
are deemed 
to 
be 
best 
practices 
on 
an 
intemational 
basis. 
This 
approach 
will 
guarantee 
that the project’s technical 
solutions 
are 
viable 
on 
the 
Bahian 
context. 
Some 
of 
the 
new

included

are 
the

i) 
strengthening 
of 
the 
Pacto 
de 
AtenqEo 
Bdsica 
- 
this 
is 
assessed 
as 
feasible 
given 
that the 
Pacto 
is 
already 
in 
place 
and 
the strong federal 
and 
state 
commitment 
to 
make 
them work. 
The 
use 
of 
similar 
performance 
agreements 
has 
been 
successfully 
supported 
by 
Bank 
projects 
in 
Bolivia 
and 
Peru; 
ii) 
organizing 
procurement/distribution 
of 
drugs 
through 
new 
arrangements 
that 
optimize 
price 
and quality 
of 
drugs 
with 
low 
administrative/transactional 
costs 
based 
on 
e-procurement. 
Assessed 
as 
feasible based 
on 
the successful experience 
of 
Brazil 
at 
the 
federal 
level 
and 
the 
experience 
of 
a 
similar 
Bank-supported

in

iii) 
promoting

to 
improve 
equity 
and

of

systems. 
This 
is

as 
risky, 
but 
feasible 
given 
the 
high

by

to 
this

and 
iv)

to

care 
(already 
discussed 
on

leamed).

4. 
Institutional: 
4.1 
Executing 
agencies: 
In 
1999, 
the 
State 
Secretariat 
of 
Health 
(SESAB)  began 
a process 
of 
self-restructuring 
to 
respond 
to 
the 
new 
sector  challenges. 
A 
new 
structure was 
put 
in 
place 
comprising 
a 
General 
Directorate 
and 
four 
Superintendencies. 
The 
organizational 
structure 
was 
simplified 
and 
its 
main 
functions 
are 
expected 
to 
change 
from 
service 
provision 
to 
policy 
making,  regulation,  technical 
assistance 
to 
municipalities, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation, 
and 
permanent  education 
and 
communication 
on 
health 
related 
matters. 
During 
project 
preparation, 
an institutional 
assessment 
was 
carried out 
to 
determine 
if 
the 
Government, 
in 
particular 
the 
SESAB, 
have the 
required 
capacity 
to 
carry 
out 
the 
project. 
The 
assessment 
included 
analysis 
of 
the 
regulatory, 
procurement, 
financial,  and organizational 
aspects 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
main 
conclusions 
of 
the 
assessments are 
the 
following: 
i) 
there 
is 
strong 
coherence 
and 
consistency 
between 
the  proposed 
project 
and 
the 
SESAB’s 
new 
institutional 
objectives  and priorities; 
ii) 
the 
regulatory 
instruments 
required 
for 
project implementation 
are 
already 
in 
place; 
and 
iii) 
the 
SESAB 
has 
the 
potential 
to 
lead 
the 
project 
to 
a  successful 
conclusion 
provided 
it 
addresses 
some 
current  technical 
and 
administrative 
weaknesses. 
Specialized 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
included 
in 
the 
project 
will 
be 
key 
to 
strengthening 
these areas. 
4.2 
Project 
management: 
A 
Project 
Coordination 
Unit 
(PCU) 
was created 
in 
2002 
to 
support 
project 
preparation. 
No 
later 
than 
three 
months 
after 
effectiveness 
it 
will 
hire 
additional 
staff 
needed 
for 
project 
implementation. 
Execution 
of 
project 
components 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
directly 
by 
the 
SESAB’s 
line 
agencies 
and 
by 
participating 
municipalities. 
The 
PCU 
will 
establish the 
project policies 
and 
make 
key 
decisions 
as 
needed. 
It 
will 
be 
staffed 
with 
high 
level 
professionals, 
especially 
in 
areas 
where 
SESAB 
is 
more 
deficient. 
A 
financial 
management system 
is 
under development 
to 
report 
expenditures 
and 
progress 
of 
project 
activities. 
In 
accordance 
with 
arrangements 
for 
Bank-financed 
projects 
in 
Brazil, 
annual 
audits 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
“Tribunal 
de 
Contas 
do Estado da 
Bahia”. 
Supervision missions 
will 
be 
carried out 
twice 
a year. 
The 
Project’s operational 
manual 
will 
describe the procedures 
to 
be 
followed 
if 
subprojects 
are 
requested 
by 
any 
of 
the 
16 
municipalities 
with 
indigenous populations. 
4.3 
Procurement 
issues: 
A 
procurement capacity 
assessment 
of 
SESAB 
was 
carried out 
in 
August 
2002 
and 
rated 
the procurement 
risk 
as 
average. 
The 
analysis 
did 
not 
detect 
any 
critical 
deficiencies that 
need 
to 
be 
addressed 
before 
project 
launch 
except 
for 
setting 
up 
of 
a 
procurement 
unit. 
The preliminary 
procurement 
plan 
reveals the 
need 
to 
procure 
more 
than 
100 
significant 
contracts; 
this 
contracting 
activity 
would 
require 
at least 
three 
full 
time 
procurement 
staff. 
SESAB 
appears 
to 
be 
very 
consistent 
on 
the 
application 
of 
the 
rules 
for 
the 
procurement 
of 
goods 
and 
services. 
Since the 
state 
secretariat 
is 
responsible 
for 
the 
supply 
of 
the goods 
necessary 
for 
the 
operation 
of 
100 
health facilities 
there 
is 
a 
good tradition 
on 
government procurement,  stock 
management 
and 
distribution 
of 
goods 
throughout 
the 
state. 
One 
annual 
ex-post 
review 
of 
procurement 
is 
recommended. 
The 
risk 
on 
procurement 
is 
increased 
by 
the Basic  Care 
Expansion 
subcomponent 
that 
will 
require 
a 
closer 
supervision 
by 
the 
Bank 
and  independent  reviewers. 
The 
actual 
needs 
of 
the 
beneficiary 
municipality 
will 
be 
defined 
on 
a 
procurement 
plan 
accompanying the 
Basic 
Care 
expansion 
plan. 
It 
is 
expected that 
a 
substantial 
share 
of 
the 
transfer 
to 
the 
beneficiary municipalities 
would 
be 
to 
finance 
incentives 
of 
the 
family 
health 
teams. 
There 
are 
several 
regimes 
for 
hiring 
these 
workers,  cooperative, 
24 
Page  25
CLT, 
etc. 
All 
of 
them 
would 
qualify 
as 
hiring 
of 
individuals 
below 
<US$35,000 
or 
in 
accordance 
with 
par. 
3.19 
of 
the Consultants 
Guidelines 
as 
explained 
in 
the 
Annex 
6 
of 
the 
PAD. 
Based 
on 
the 
finding 
of

assessment,

plan 
was agreed 
with 
the 
borrower 
in 
eight 
areas 
that 
cover

local

4.4 
Financial 
management 
issues: 
A 
Financial 
Management 
Assessment 
was 
conducted 
in 
August 
2002. 
While 
the 
country 
specific 
risk 
and 
the 
state 
of 
Bahia 
risks 
are 
medium, 
and 
while 
SESAB 
has 
participated 
as 
collaborating 
agency 
in 
three 
Bank 
projects,  the 
assessment 
rated 
the 
project

as 
high, 
based 
on: 
(i) 
SESAB’s relative 
inexperience 
with 
Bank 
procedures; 
(ii) 
the 
fact 
that 
the 
PCU 
was  established 
only 
in 
2002, 
(iii) 
the 
information 
system 
had 
not 
been 
created, 
and 
(iv) 
the 
Bank’s 
lack 
of 
experience 
with 
the proposed mechanism 
of 
disbursements 
for 
subprojects. 
An 
action 
plan 
was agreed 
with 
the 
Borrower.  The Bahia 
project 
may 
benefit 
from 
the  experience 
gained 
by 
the 
Bank 
in 
supervising 
the 
Family 
Health 
Extension 
Project, 
which 
is 
initiating 
the 
use 
of 
SOTS 
for 
subproject 
disbursements. 
The 
FM 
assessment 
recommends that 
FM 
staff 
supervise the 
project 
three times 
a year. 
By 
law, 
the annual 
financial 
audit reports 
of 
the 
project 
accounts 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
the 
Tribunal 
de 
Contas, 
this 
was 
found 
acceptable 
to 
the 
Bank. 
5. 
Environmental: 
5.1 
Summarize 
the 
steps 
undertaken 
for 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
EMP 
preparation 
(including 
consultation and 
disclosure) 
and 
the 
signiflcant 
issues 
and their 
treatment 
emerging 
from 
this 
analysis. 
Environmental 
Category: 
B 
(Partial 
Assessment) 
An 
environmental 
assessment 
was 
conducted 
in 
August 
2002 
in 
order 
to: 
(i) 
carry 
out 
an 
environmental 
review 
of 
the 
project 
ant 
its 
institutional 
arrangements; 
(ii) 
assess 
local 
environmental 
guidelines 
for 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
medical 
waste 
management; 
(iii) 
compile 
existing 
documentation 
pertaining 
local 
norms 
and 
legislation  regulating 
medical 
waste 
management; 
and 
(iv) 
draft 
an 
Environmental 
Management 
Plan 
(EMP) 
for 
the 
project. 
The 
assessment 
concluded  that the 
State 
of 
Bahia’s  environmental regulations and 
norms 
contain 
clear procedures 
and 
guidelines 
for 
environmental 
assessment 
of 
health 
services, 
proper 
handling 
of 
medical 
wastes, 
proper 
final 
disposition 
of 
such 
wastes, 
and proper 
monitoring 
guidelines 
for 
reducing 
health 
risks. 
State 
and 
municipal 
roles 
are 
clearly 
stated 
as 
well. 
In 
addition, 
results 
from 
field 
visits 
to 
health 
centers 
in 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia 
indicate 
that 
local 
sanitary 
and environmental 
practices 
for 
proper 
handling 
and 
disposal 
of 
medical 
wastes 
are 
applied; 
however, 
implementation 
of 
these 
norms and 
standards 
in 
poor 
rural 
municipalities 
may 
be 
weak. 
Some 
concern remains 
as 
these 
municipalities 
do 
not 
have 
the technical capacity 
to 
design 
and carry out proper 
waste management plans, 
including 
the 
proper 
management 
and 
final 
disposal 
of 
medical 
wastes. 
Based 
on 
these 
findings 
the 
EA 
recommended 
an Environmental 
Category 
B 
rating. 
Most 
of 
the 
potential 
environmental 
impacts 
associated 
with 
the 
project 
are 
considered positive. 
Project 
components 
are 
aimed 
at 
increasing 
efficiency 
of 
hospital 
care, 
reducing 
wasteful 
health 
care 
practices 
and 
better 
use 
of 
resources. 
However, 
even 
though 
the 
proposed Project 
does 
not 
have 
a 
direct 
impact 
on 
the 
environment, 
the 
implementation 
of 
health 
services 
and 
the 
generation 
of 
medical 
wastes 
as 
these 
health 
centers 
provide 
better 
services, 
might 
have 
a 
direct 
connotation 
with 
environmental implications. 
During 
pre-appraisal, 
the 
Bank 
and 
SESAB 
teams 
discussed 
the 
main 
findings 
of 
the 
EAR 
and 
agreed 
with 
the 
proposed 
EMP. 
Counterpart 
team 
agreed 
that  the 
Project’s 
Operations 
Manual 
and Project 
Implementation 
Plan 
would 
include 
the 
Environmental 
Management Plan. 
All 
actions 
are 
considered 
within 
the 
scope 
of 
the 
project’s components and 
will 
be 
the 
responsibility 
of 
SESAB. 
No 
additional 
staff 
25 
Page  26
is 
required 
for 
the 
monitoring 
of 
the 
plan. 
For 
the 
provision 
of 
municipal 
assistance, 
it 
was 
agreed 
the 
project 
would 
reserve 
up 
to 
10% 
of 
the cost 
for 
component 
2 
of 
the 
Project. 
No 
additional 
studies 
are 
needed 
for 
the 
preparation 
of 
the 
project. 
5.2 
What 
are 
the 
main 
features 
of 
the 
EMP 
and 
are 
they 
adequate? 
The 
Project's 
EMP 
will 
strengthen the 
environmental 
management 
capacity 
of 
SESAB 
as 
well 
as 
the 
environmental 
management capacities 
of 
participating 
center 
municipalities. 
EMP 
activities include: 
(i) 
Implementation 
of 
existing 
procedures 
for local 
environmental 
assessment 
and licensing 
of 
health 
centers; 
(ii) 
Preparation 
of 
Medical 
Waste  Plans 
in 
each 
health 
center 
of 
participating 
municipalities; 
(iii) 
Municipal 
technical 
assistance 
for 
the 
preparation 
of 
Solid 
Waste management 
plans, 
including 
medical 
wastes, 
with 
emphasis 
in 
poor 
participating municipalities  covering  over 
200 
beds  equivalent; 
(iv) 
Priority 
access 
to 
sub-projects 
to 
be 
given 
to 
municipalities 
with 
proper 
solid 
waste management plans; 
(v) 
Implementation 
of 
environmental 
criteria 
in 
the 
audit 
of 
health 
services, 
according 
to 
existing 
DivisZo 
de 
Vigildncia 
Sanitaria 
(DIVISA) 
guidelines; 
(vi) 
Implementation 
of 
local 
basic 
sanitation 
information, 
through 
the 
financing 
of 
information 
material, 
according 
to 
local 
existing 
guidelines; 
and 
(vi) 
Implementation 
of 
environmental 
criteria 
during 
construction 
and 
supervision 
of 
infrastructure,  where 
neededwhere 
applicable. 
This 
plan 
was 
formalized 
by 
a 
letter 
dated 
May 
7, 
2003. 
5.3 
For 
Category 
A 
and

of

5.4 
How

and proposed environment

plan?  Describe 
mechanisms

and

The 
assessment 
included

to primary

to

in

-

for

in

and 
Camaqari. 
5.5 
What 
mechanisms 
have been 
established 
to monitor 
and 
evaluate 
the 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
on 
the 
environment'? 
Do 
the 
indicators reflect 
the 
objectives 
and 
results 
of 
the 
EMP'? 
Environmental  Management 
Plan 
implementation  and 
monitoring 
will 
require 
close 
supervision, 
particularly 
during 
the 
first 
two 
years 
of 
the Project. 
SESAB 
will 
include 
in 
the 
Project's  Operation 
Manual 
detailed 
procedures 
and criteria 
according 
to 
the 
activities 
and 
scope 
of 
the 
EMP. 
Project 
implementation 
unit 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
reporting 
implementation 
of 
the 
EMP. 
Progress reports 
will 
include: 
(i) 
Progress 
in 
the 
execution 
of 
each one 
of 
the activities 
of 
technical 
assistance 
and investment 
proposed 
in 
the 
plan; 
(ii) 
The impact 
indicators 
by 
component 
will 
be defined 
in 
function 
of 
the 
number 
of 
activities 
of 
each 
component 
performed 
within 
the 
framework 
of 
this 
environment 
management 
plan. 
Number 
of 
health 
centers 
with 
simplified 
environmental 
licenses at 
end 
of 
year 
1, 
year 
to 
and 
year 3 
plus 
number 
of 
municipalities 
with 
solid 
waste 
management 
plans 
at 
end 
of 
the project should 
be 
reported. 
These 
indicators 
will 
be 
applied 
on 
the 
evaluation 
phase, 
time 
at 
which 
real 
impact 
will 
be 
identified, 
positive 
as 
well 
as 
negative, 
accomplished 
and 
ones 
not 
foreseen, 
to 
finally 
make 
the 
necessary 
recommendations; 
(iii) 
In

Implementation 
of 
environmental criteria 
in 
the

will 
reflect
