6. 
Social: 
6.1 
Summarize 
key 
social 
issues 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
objectives, 
and 
specify  the  project’s 
social 
development 
outcomes. 
A 
social 
assessment 
(SA) 
was 
carried 
out 
as 
a 
joint 
effort 
of 
SESAB 
and 
the 
World 
Bank 
as 
an 
iterative 
process 
of 
systematic 
stakeholder consultations 
and 
participation 
to 
be 
continued through 
implementation. 
Several 
instruments commissioned 
by 
SESAB 
were used 
for 
the social analysis 
of 
the proposed project. 
Two 
large-scale 
field 
reviews 
of 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Family 
Health 
Program 
were conducted 
by 
the 
Federal 
University 
of 
Bahia 
in 
2000 
and 
2001. 
A 
team 
of 
anthropologists 
from 
the 
Collective 
Health 
Institute carried 
out 
a 
Study 
of 
Social 
Aspects 
of 
the 
Health 
Reform 
Program 
in 
the  State 
of 
Bahia 
(Beneficiary 
Assessment). 
A 
Gender 
Issues 
and 
Recommendations 
(April 
2000) 
and 
a 
Brazil 
Gender 
Review 
were carried 
out 
by 
LCSPR, 
World 
Bank 
(January 
2000). 
Field 
visits 
and further  consultation 
with 
Municipal 
Health 
Secretaries, 
Municipal 
Health Council 
members, 
members 
of 
the 
Community 
Health 
Agents 
Program 
(PACS) 
medical 
doctors, nurses 
and 
PSF, 
were carried 
out 
during 
field 
visits 
at 
the 
time 
of 
project 
preparation. 
Although 
still 
in 
infancy,  PSF 
is 
widely 
accepted 
by 
State  Secretariats, 
mayors, 
Municipal 
Health 
Councils, 
medical 
doctors, 
nurses 
and beneficiaries. 
Some 
of 
the 
lessons 
learned 
from 
PACS/PSF 
implementation 
are: 
(i) 
Access 
to 
services 
is 
not 
adequate 
as 
most 
PSFs are 
in 
urban 
areas 
while 
over 
60% 
of 
the 
population 
is 
in 
rural 
areas, 
(ii) 
Infrastructural 
arrangements 
must 
be 
in 
place 
at 
time 
of 
staffing 
health 
units 
under 
PACUPSF, 
(iii) 
Incentives 
to 
attract 
medical 
staff to 
work 
in 
rural 
areas 
must 
include 
training 
in 
community mobilization 
and 
health 
maintenance, 
(iv) 
Competition 
of 
nurses 
and 
community 
health 
agents 
is 
due 
to 
salary 
inequity, 
(v) 
There 
are 
varied 
opinions  among beneficiaries 
about 
the 
desirability 
of 
traditional 
methods 
of 
healing(such 
as 
“rezadeiras”, 
traditional 
“rezadores” 
of 
Catholic 
influence, 
“curadores” 
oriented 
by 
Afro-Brazilian 
cults, 
“espiritas” 
(spiritual 
counselors, 
mediums). 
Many 
of 
these 
practices 
are 
part 
of 
Candomble‘, 
Macumba 
or 
other 
Afro-Brazilian 
healing 
tradition). 
It 
is 
recommended that 
traditional 
approaches 
to 
health be 
included 
in 
the 
initial 
household 
survey 
and 
the 
original 
diagnosis 
of 
the regions. Participatory 
processes 
and 
systematic consultations 
need 
to 
continue 
through 
implementation 
to 
ensure 
high 
impact 
on 
health 
indicators. 
Care 
must 
be 
taken, 
though, 
to 
not 
engage 
project 
activities 
with 
specific 
political 
agendas. 
Gender 
Issues 
and Recommendations 
(April 
2000) 
and the 
Brazil 
Gender 
Review 
(January 
2000) 
present 
important 
data 
and recommendations 
related 
to 
women’s 
and child’s 
health. 
These 
studies 
point 
out 
that 
inadequate 
pre-natal 
care 
persists 
and  the 
proportion 
of 
at-risk 
pregnancies 
and 
matemal 
mortality 
continues 
to 
be 
elevated, 
which 
suggests 
serious 
problems 
with 
the 
health 
care 
system 
in 
the proposed 
project 
area. 
It 
recommends 
the 
inclusion 
of 
men 
in 
reproductive health 
programs 
to 
educate 
males 
on 
the 
risk 
of 
contracting 
AIDS 
and 
other 
STDs. 
Among 
other 
recommendations, 
it 
suggests 
that 
the 
project 
look 
at general 
public 
health 
issues 
in 
a 
gendered 
way, 
which 
includes 
men 
participation 
in 
family 
and 
reproductive health 
programs. 
The 
social  development 
outcomes 
of 
the 
project 
will 
consist 
of 
increased 
access 
to 
health 
care 
by 
previously 
excluded 
populations and reduced 
inequality 
in 
the 
distribution 
of 
public 
resources. 
The 
state 
of 
Bahia 
comprises 
4 
17 
municipalities 
out 
of 
which 
14 
include 
Indigenous 
Peoples clustered 
in 
12 
ethnic 
groups, 
numbering 
around 
13,000 
people. 
They 
are 
located 
in 
the 
north, 
west 
and 
south 
of 
the 
State. 
They 
represent 
a 
tiny 
fraction 
of 
the 
state 
total 
(less 
than 
0.1%), 
though they 
rank 
among 
the 
poorest 
segments 
of 
this 
population. 
Within 
Brazil’s 
legal 
framework, 
health 
care 
of 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
is 
under 
the 
Federal Government’s 
jurisdiction, 
to 
be 
implemented 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
through 
FUNASA 
with 
the 
States 
and 
27 
Page  28
Municipalities 
only 
in 
a 
complementary 
role. 
The 
Bank 
is 
currently  supporting 
FUNASA 
through 
the 
VIGISUS 
Project, 
an 
APL 
whose 
first 
phase 
has 
been extended 
until 
December 
31, 
2003, 
and 
whose 
second 
phase 
is 
currently 
under 
preparation. 
During 
preparation 
of 
the 
VIGISUS 
project 
a 
nationwide 
Indigenous 
People’s 
Development 
Plan 
was prepared 
and 
discussed 
with 
Indigenous Groups and 
peoples. 
The 
project 
includes 
an 
Indigenous 
Health 
subcomponent that 
finances 
health 
care 
adapted 
to 
the 
needs 
and culture 
of 
the 
Indigenous 
people. 
FUNASA’s 
progress 
and 
plans 
for 
the 
state 
of 
Bahia 
can 
be 
seen 
in 
www.funasa.gov.br/ind/pdfs/dsei09.pdf., 
which 
includes 
a 
description 
of 
investments 
in 
staff, equipment 
and works 
by 
indigenous district. 
The 
proposed 
Bahia Health 
Project includes 
a 
component 
to 
finance 
Basic 
Health 
Care 
subprojects 
at the 
municipal 
level. 
Given 
their  small 
representation 
and 
their 
existing 
basic 
care 
and 
epidemiological 
coverage 
offered 
by 
FUNASA 
and 
VIGISUS 
outside the Project, 
it 
is 
highly 
unlikely 
that Indigenous 
peoples 
will 
need 
to 
opt 
for 
basic coverage 
under 
this 
operation. 
Hence 
OD 
4.20 
is 
deemed 
not 
to 
apply. 
Nevertheless, 
in 
the 
remote 
future 
eventuality 
that a 
subproject 
in 
an

with 
an 
Indigenous 
Population 
could 
be 
submitted 
for

(IPDF) 
(Annex 
11) 
sets 
the  process 
for 
the

would

to

Within

whenever 
Indigenous 
People 
are 
to 
be included 
as 
possible beneficiaries 
of 
the 
activities financed under 
this

(as

the existence 
of

People 
in

of 
the 
municipality 
submitted 
for

Municipality 
with 
the

and 
in

with 
the 
State 
will 
prepare 
a 
subproject 
level 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
Development 
Plan 
(IPDP) 
that 
will 
address 
the 
health 
needs 
of 
those 
people 
in 
a 
culturally 
adequate 
manner 
as 
required 
by 
OD 
4.20. 
The 
PDF 
will 
be 
included 
in 
the 
Operational 
Manual, 
and 
will 
describe 
the specific procedures 
to 
be 
followed 
in 
the event 
that 
a 
proposed 
subproject 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
carried 
out 
in 
an 
area 
where Indigenous People 
reside.  These 
will 
include 
the 
need 
for 
consultations 
with 
affected 
groups 
and 
arrangements 
for 
working 
with 
Federal 
agencies, 
especially, 
FUNASA 
to 
cover the 
health 
care needs 
of 
this 
population. 
The 
subproject 
level 
IPDP 
will 
include 
a 
description 
of 
institutional 
capacity 
for 
addressing 
Indigenous 
People’s 
issues 
at the 
local 
level, 
an implementation 
schedule, 
the need 
for  monitoring 
and 
evaluating 
subproject 
activities 
benefiting 
FUNASA 
groups, 
if 
any, 
and the relevant 
cost 
estimates 
and 
financing 
plan 
(including 
Bank 
loan 
proceeds) 
and 
for 
disclosure 
of 
the 
IPDP 
in 
accordance 
with 
Bank 
policy. 
Bank 
approval 
of 
the 
subproject 
level 
IPDP 
and related 
institutional 
agreements 
will 
be 
a 
condition 
of 
disbursement 
against 
subprojects 
benefiting 
Indigenous 
Groups.

6.3 
How 
does 
the 
project 
involve 
consultations 
or  collaboration 
with 
NGOs 
or 
other 
civil 
society 
organizations? 
28 
Page  29
By 
contrast 
to 
other 
Latin 
American 
countries, 
the 
Brazilian 
health 
system places 
a 
great 
emphasis 
on 
participation 
by 
civil 
society. 
The 
organization 
of 
SUS 
includes 
Health 
Councils 
at 
every 
level 
(National, 
State, 
Municipal 
and 
Local). 
These 
Health 
Councils 
include 
representatives 
of 
civil 
society (providers 
and 
users) 
and have 
the 
authority 
to 
approve 
all 
plans 
and 
budgets 
and 
to 
request any 
information 
about 
the 
management 
of 
health 
budgets. 
The 
Councils 
will 
have 
a 
significant 
role 
in 
the 
project, 
by 
approving 
all 
sub-project  proposals 
and 
by 
making 
part 
of 
the 
govemance 
of 
micro-regions. 
Councils 
have 
a 
relatively 
short 
existence, 
and  their 
impact 
on 
decision 
making 
in 
the 
sector 
is 
not 
yet 
sufficiently 
understood;  a 
study 
on 
the 
ethnic, 
social and 
gender 
composition 
of 
the councils 
is 
under 
way 
and 
its 
results 
(expected 
in 
mid-2003) 
may 
conclude 
that 
some 
project 
activities  should 
be 
used 
to 
further 
develop 
participation 
in 
Bahia. 
Also 
in 
contrast 
to 
other 
Latin 
American 
countries, 
Bahia 
has 
seemingly 
no 
studies 
about the 
health 
status 
and 
the 
level 
of 
access 
and 
satisfaction 
of 
its 
ethnic 
groups. 
The 
household 
surveys 
will 
provide 
a 
baseline 
and 
a measure 
of 
progress 
in 
this 
account. 
6.4 
What 
institutional 
arrangements 
have  been 
provided  to 
ensure 
the 
project 
achieves 
its 
social 
development 
outcomes'? 
Municipal 
Health 
councils 
will 
review--and 
may 
veto--all 
subproject proposals. 
The 
state 
Health 
Council 
will 
approve the 
annual 
budget 
for 
the 
project,  and 
may 
request 
any 
additional 
information 
about 
the 
annual 
plan 
of 
operations. 
The 
ongoing 
study 
on 
the make-up 
of 
these 
councils 
will 
make additional 
recommendations 
conceming 
ways 
to 
give 
a 
voice 
to 
vulnerable 
groups 
of 
the population. 
In 
the event 
that 
subprojects 
submitted 
for 
financing 
under 
the 
Basic Care component 
are 
located 
in 
a 
municipality 
where 
Indigenous 
People 
reside, 
the 
municipality, 
with 
SESAB's 
assistance 
will 
work 
closely 
with 
FUNASA 
to 
ensure 
its 
active 
participation 
in 
the 
design and 
implementation 
of 
subproject 
level 
IPDPs, 
in 
the  consultation 
process 
with 
the 
Indigenous  Groups 
involved,  and 
in 
the 
design 
of 
activities that 
address 
the 
health 
care 
needs 
of 
the 
affected 
group. 
To 
ensure 
adequate 
cooperation, 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia 
will 
enter 
into 
a 
cooperation 
agreement 
with 
FUNASA 
before commencing 
preparation 
of 
any 
IPDP. 
The 
process 
in 
the 
IPDF 
will 
be 
followed 
(Annex 
11). 
6.5 
How 
will 
the 
project 
monitor 
performance 
in 
terms 
of 
social development 
outcomes? 
Increased 
access 
will 
be 
monitored 
with 
the 
10 
key 
performance 
indicators 
of 
annex 
1. 
While 
there 
will 
be 
no 
periodic 
way 
of 
monitoring 
that 
the 
improvement 
in 
these 
indicators 
is 
reaching 
the 
previously 
excluded 
populations,  there 
is 
a 
strong 
presumption 
that  any 
progress 
in 
these 
indicators 
will 
be 
concentrated 
among the 
excluded 
(because 
the rest 
of 
the 
population 
already 
has 
access 
to 
basic 
services, 
hence 
any expansion 
will 
happen among 
the 
excluded). 
Furthermore, the comparison 
of 
the 
first 
and 
second surveys 
will 
allow 
an 
evaluation 
to 
confirm or 
reject 
this 
presumption. 
APL2 
will 
include 
a 
design 
that responds 
to 
the results 
of 
this 
evaluation. 
It 
should 
be 
noticed 
that 
the proposed 
approach 
monitors 
outputs 
and 
not 
processes. 
However, 
in 
order 
to 
reach 
the 
proposed 
outputs, 
it 
will 
be 
necessary 
to 
use 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
previous 
paragraphs 
(conceming 
stakeholder 
participation, 
traditional 
practices 
and 
gender 
features). 
Improved 
equity 
will 
be 
measured 
on 
a 
yearly 
basis based 
on 
data 
from 
executed budgets.

The 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
policy 
does 
not 
apply 
because 
it 
is 
unlikely 
that 
the 
municipalities 
where 
indigenous peoples 
are 
residing 
(of 
which 
there 
are 
very 
few) 
will 
opt 
for 
basic 
health 
care 
under 
the 
mechanisms 
put 
in 
place 
by 
the 
project.

indigenous 
peoples 
of 
Bahia 
are 
being 
served 
by 
an existing 
targeted 
national 
indigenous 
health 
care 
structure managed 
by 
FUNASA.

a 
precautionary 
measure, 
in 
the 
event 
any 
of 
these 
municipalities 
proposes 
to 
carry 
out 
a 
basic 
care 
subproject 
that 
could 
affect 
the 
indigenous peoples 
living 
there, 
an 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
Framework 
(mentioned 
in 
section 
6 
and 
described 
in 
Annex 
11) 
will 
apply, 
in 
order 
to 
ensure 
that 
the subproject 
is 
prepared and 
implemented 
in 
a 
culturally 
appropriate 
manner. 
Under 
the 
Indigenous 
Peoples 
Framework,

Peoples 
Development 
Plans 
(IPDPs) 
will 
be

and

IPDPs

be 
approved 
by 
the 
Bank prior to

of 
the respective 
subproject, 
and disclosed 
in 
country 
and 
in 
the 
Infoshop 
as 
per 
Bank 
policy.

F. 
Sustainability 
and Risks 
1. 
Sustainability: 
Brazilian 
health 
expenditures 
have been 
growing 
at a 
rapid 
rate 
and 
are 
now 
at a 
high 
level for 
Latin 
America. One 
of 
the 
objectives 
of 
decentralizing 
health 
care 
in 
Brazil 
is 
to 
improve 
the sustainability 
of 
the 
health 
system 
by 
imposing  hard 
budget 
constraints 
on 
budget 
holders  (previously 
the 
state- 
government, 
today 
municipalities 
and micro-regions) 
and 
by 
increasing the 
share 
of 
financing 
provided 
by 
sub-national 
governments  (to 
be 
achieved 
by 
a 
combination 
of 
federal 
matching 
grants 
and 
other 
incentives 
and 
the 
implementation 
of 
Constitutional Amendment 
29 
which 
mandates 
minimum 
levels 
of 
spending 
in 
health). 
The 
APL 
will 
contribute 
to 
implementing 
these 
policies, 
hence; 
the 
APL 
would 
contribute 
to 
the  sustainability 
of 
the 
Bahian 
health 
system. 
The 
main 
risk 
at 
this 
level 
is 
that  the 
Municipality 
of 
Salvador 
(which 
today 
assigns 
to 
health 
only 
a 
third 
of 
what 
it 
is 
required 
according 
to 
Constitutional Amendment 
29) 
refuses 
to 
take 
charge 
of 
its 
local 
health 
system 
and 
that 
Salvador 
continue 
to 
absorb a 
growing 
share 
of 
public 
expenditures. 
At 
a 
more 
micro-level, 
one 
must 
consider 
the sustainability 
of 
the 
specific activities 
to 
be 
supported 
by 
the 
APL, 
and 
here, 
there 
are 
significant 
risks. 
Multi-municipal 
micro-regions 
are 
intrinsically 
unstable 
because 
municipal 
politics lead 
to 
a 
desire 
for 
local 
level 
investments. 
APLI 
is 
designed 
to 
support 
the 
exploration 
of 
governance 
systems 
to 
overcome 
those tendencies. 
An 
additional 
challenge 
at 
this 
level 
is 
the 
likely 
cost 
of 
implementing 
management structures 
- 
internationally, 
these 
are 
known 
to 
have 
the 
potential 
for 
increased 
efficiency, 
but 
to 
require 
high 
administrative 
costs 
in 
the 
range 
of 
10% 
to 
20% 
of 
30 
Page  31
health 
expenditures. 
The 
APL 
will 
finance 
the 
initial 
cost 
of 
these 
management 
structures 
(including 
substantial up-start counterpart 
financing 
from 
the 
state 
government), 
but 
their 
future 
cost 
is 
unknown. 
Most 
municipal 
sub-projects 
for 
the expansion 
of 
Basic 
Care 
will 
lead 
to 
higher 
recurrent 
costs. 
For 
most 
municipalities, 
this 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
sustainable, 
given 
that 
Constitutional Amendment 
29 
requires 
a 
higher allocation 
of 
resources 
for 
health 
than 
what 
is 
presently 
available 
-- 
hence 
the 
sub-project 
will 
finance 
a 
rapid 
increase 
in 
the 
level 
of 
expenditures  that 
will 
then 
be 
sustained  based 
on 
local 
contributions. 
There remains 
a 
special 
concern 
about the 
sustainability 
of 
Family 
Health 
teams 
- 
as 
explained 
above, 
their 
cost 
per beneficiary 
is 
highest 
in 
remote 
municipalities and 
the 
existing 
federal 
subsidy 
is 
far 
short 
of 
the cost 
--their sustainability 
may 
require 
a 
permanent 
increase 
in 
transfers 
from 
the 
federal 
or 
state-government 
(because 
of 
this 
risk, 
sub-projects 
for  family 
health 
teams 
will 
only 
be 
implemented 
where 
a 
credible sustainability 
plan 
exists).

3. 
Possible 
Controversial 
Aspects: 
The municipality 
of 
Salvador 
has 
agreed 
to 
develop the capacity 
to 
acquire 
Gest6o 
Plena, 
and 
APL1 
will 
provide 
technical 
assistance 
to 
this 
end. 
As 
the 
municipality 
is 
autonomous, 
the 
state 
government cannot 
guarantee 
that 
the 
municipality 
will 
effectively 
acquire 
Gestiio 
Plena. 
In 
the event 
that 
the 
municipality 
decides 
not 
to 
make the 
change, 
the 
Bank 
and 
the 
government 
will 
have 
to 
decide 
if 
APL2 
should 
still 
be 
implemented 
(i.e. 
if 
the 
program 
development objectives 
can 
still 
be 
reached).

Annex 
2: 
Detailed 
Project Description 
BRAZIL: Bahia Health System 
Reform 
Project 
Component 
I. 
Implementation 
of 
health  micro-rezions 
in 
Bahia. 
(US$ 
18.3 
million, 
including 
con 
tinBen cies) 
This 
component 
will 
strengthen 
the 
management  capacity, 
Govemance and 
resolution 
capacity 
of 
the 
participating 
microregions. 
A. 
Strengthening 
management 
and 
governance 
of 
micro-regions 
Strengthening 
the 
management 
capacity 
of 
the 
microregion 
for 
the purchase 
of 
health 
care 
services, the 
audit 
of 
the 
delivery 
of 
these 
services and the 
initiation 
of 
an 
accreditation 
function. 
To 
strengthen the 
role 
of 
the 
micro-region 
as 
a 
purchaser 
of 
health 
care 
services, 
the 
project 
will 
support: 
(i) 
the 
development and deployment 
of 
performance 
based contracts 
between 
the 
micro-regions 
and their 
local 
providers, 
and 
the 
implementation 
of 
a 
contracting 
department 
at 
the 
micro-regional level. 
(ii) 
the 
implementation 
of 
Regulation 
Centers 
with 
the capacity 
to 
authorize referrals 
for 
treatment 
and testing 
based 
on 
medical 
criteria 
and 
on 
transparent 
priorities 
and the 
introduction 
of 
medically-based 
prior 
authorization 
for 
elective 
hospital 
care; 
and 
(iii) 
the 
adaptation 
of 
SUS 
card 
technology 
to 
the 
local 
conditions 
and 
the 
implementation 
of 
applications 
that 
address 
the 
needs 
of 
microregional 
management. 
In 
order 
to 
effectively 
serve 
in 
an 
evaluative 
and 
monitoring 
capacity, 
the 
micro-regional Regulation 
Center 
will 
develop 
the 
capacity 
to 
conduct 
detailed 
clinical, 
environmental6, 
and 
financial 
audits 
of 
providers 
and 
facilities 
and 
will 
initiate 
a  process 
of 
accreditation. 
Performance-based 
contracting 
requires 
accurate data 
derived 
from 
these 
audits 
to 
determine 
if 
the 
contracted 
provider 
is 
in 
compliance 
with 
the 
contract 
goals. 
Audit 
functions 
also 
serve 
as 
a 
control 
mechanism 
to 
assure 
provider 
accountability 
for 
efficiency 
and 
quality 
of 
care, 
as 
well 
as 
to 
detect 
fraud, 
abuse, 
and ineffective 
practices. 
Accreditation 
will 
be 
based 
on 
standard 
criteria. 
The 
project 
will 
support: 
(i) 
contracting 
specialized 
consultants 
to 
provide 
SESAB 
with 
tum-key 
operations 
of 
the 
auditing 
and accreditation 
functions; 
(ii) 
promoting 
preparation 
of 
Medical 
Waste Plans 
in 
health 
care 
facilities 
and 
implementation 
of 
existing 
local 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
licensing 
of 
health 
centers; 
(iii) 
developing 
courses 
to 
teach 
the 
techniques 
of 
evaluation 
of 
patient 
care 
through 
the 
use 
of 
standards 
of 
care 
and 
clinical 
protocols,  the 
use 
of 
standard 
guidelines 
for 
accreditation 
of 
hospitals, 
and 
the 
techniques 
of 
internal 
quality 
review 
and 
the 
investigation and reporting 
of 
sentinel 
events. 
Developing 
new 
forms 
of 
Governance 
in 
the 
micro-region. 
Micro-regions 
must 
develop 
governance 
systems 
that 
balance the 
priorities, 
needs 
and 
capacities 
of 
large municipalities, 
their small 
surrounding 
municipalities 
and the 
state. 
No 
firm 
blueprint 
exists 
for 
this 
system 
of 
governance, 
and they 
will 
need 
to 
evolve 
based 
on 
trial 
and 
error. 
The 
project 
will 
support 
this 
process 
by 
various 
means, 
including 
by: 
(i) 
financing 
the 
initial 
costs 
of 
implementing 
local 
goveming 
bodies, 
(ii) 
the 
implementation 
of 
microregional 
planning 
and 
budgeting 
tools, 
(iii) 
sponsoring 
meetings and 
seminars 
for 
municipal 
health 
secretaries, 
and 
sponsoring 
field 
trips 
for 
members 
of 
the 
governing 
body 
to 
visit 
other 
states 
that 
have 
successfully 
implemented microregions. 
Outputs 
of 
this 
subcomponent 
will 
include: 
0 
0 
authorization and pre-certification 
review 
of elective 
procedures 
and 
admissions. 
Deployment 
of 
a 
new 
contract 
model 
with 
performance 
standards. 
Implementation 
of 
at 
least 
five 
Micro-regional 
Regulation 
Centers 
using 
medical 
criteria 
for 
referral 
Ensure

in 
the 
audit 
of 
health 
services, 
according 
to 
existing 
Divisao da Vigilancia 
Sanitaria 
(DIVISA) 
guidelines.

B. 
Equipment for 
the 
first 
Level 
of 
Medium 
Complexity 
Healthcare 
(Ml) 
SESAB 
has 
established 
31 
micro-regions 
in 
Bahia. 
Most 
micro-regions continue 
to 
lack 
a 
full 
array 
of 
M1 
services. 
Studies 
have been 
performed 
to 
estimate 
the 
equipment and 
service 
deficits 
in 
the 
state 
by 
micro-region,  and 
to 
plan 
for 
equipment 
investments 
in 
these 
regions. 
The 
project 
will 
finance 
equipment 
for 
micro-regions 
in 
which 
a 
municipio 
polo 
has 
met 
two 
conditions: 
(i) 
it 
has 
signed 
agreements 
with 
its 
partner 
municipalities 
to 
engage 
in 
management 
and regulation 
of 
the 
micro-regional 
healthcare 
network 
through 
its 
Micro-Regional 
Regulation 
Center, 
and 
(ii) 
it 
has 
developed 
a 
work 
plan 
to 
achieve 
full 
management 
capacity 
(Gestao 
Plena 
Municipa)l 
within 
three 
years. 
Financial 
support 
will 
be 
provided 
for 
purchase 
of 
equipment 
in 
the 
following 
categories: 
minor 
surgical 
suites, 
urgent 
care 
centers, 
ultrasound, 
radiography, 
clinical 
laboratory, 
small 
vehicles 
for 
transport 
of 
specimens, 
and 
vans 
for 
transportation 
of 
patients 
(located at the 
municipio 
polo). 
Financing 
will 
also 
support 
the purchase 
of 
vehicles 
to 
transport patients 
referred 
within 
the 
micro-region 
and 
to 
transport 
specimens 
from 
laboratory 
collection 
stations 
to 
the 
contracted  laboratory. 
These 
vehicles 
will 
also 
be 
used 
to 
support 
the 
distribution network 
in 
the 
micro-region 
for 
the 
basic pharmacy program. 
Outputs

C. 
Control 
and 
Management 
of 
High 
Complexity 
Care 
This 
sub-component 
will 
strengthen the 
capacity 
of 
SESAB 
as 
a 
purchaser 
and 
auditor 
of 
high 
complexity 
care 
and 
will 
initiate 
accreditation activities 
for 
high 
complexity 
providers. 
Introduce 
Performance 
contracting: 
New 
models 
of 
contracting, 
providing 
incentives 
for 
improved 
provider 
performance  and 
specifying 
penalties 
for 
poor 
performance, 
are 
essential 
to 
a  strategy 
of 
enhancing 
quality 
and 
efficiency. 
Performance indicators 
will 
include 
measures 
of 
access, 
availability, 
patient 
satisfaction, 
quality 
of 
care, 
utilization 
management, 
and  results 
of 
investigation 
of 
patient 
complaints and 
grievances, 
among 
others. 
In 
these 
contracts, 
providers 
and 
facilities 
will 
be 
mandated 
to 
participate 
in 
utilization 
review, 
quality 
improvement, 
and reporting 
activities. 
Deployment 
of 
a 
Regulation 
Center 
for 
High 
Complexity 
Care 
at 
the 
state 
level: 
SESAB 
is 
currently 
implementing 
a 
Central 
de 
RegulaqLo 
for 
high 
complexity 
care. 
The 
center 
will 
regulate 
high 
complexity 
services 
that 
are 
delivered 
throughout Bahia. 
SESABs immediate proposal 
is 
to 
regulate hemodialysis, 
AIDS 
therapies, 
high-risk 
obstetrics/ neonatology, oncology, 
and in-patient 
rehabilitation 
care 
through 
the 
center, 
adding additional 
services 
over 
time. 
This 
center 
will 
evaluate 
requests 
for 
high 
complexity 
services 
and 
will 
distribute referrals 
for 
high 
complexity 
care 
in 
an 
equitable and 
transparent 
manner. 
Strengthen 
audit 
functions 
in 
high 
complexity care. 
Performance-based 
contracting 
requires 
accurate 
data 
derived 
from 
these 
audits 
in 
order 
to 
determine 
if 
the  contracted 
provider 
is 
in 
compliance 
with 
the 
contract 
goals. 
Audit 
functions 
also 
serve 
as 
a 
control mechanism 
to 
assure 
provider 
accountability 
for 
efficiency 
and 
quality 
of 
care, 
as 
well 
as 
to 
detect 
fraud, 
abuse, 
and ineffective 
 practice^.^ 
Because 
’ 
Examples 
of 
audits 
include: 
(i) 
monitoring 
of 
provider 
performance 
of 
indicators specified 
in 
contracts. 
(ii) 
hospital 
audits: 
concurrent 
utilization 
review 
of 
hospital 
admissions 
and 
retrospective 
reviews 
of 
selected 
cases 
after 
discharge. 
(iii) 
systematic 
review 
of 
ambulatory 
procedures 
and 
ancillary 
procedures: 
including 
site 
visits 
to 
high 
volume 
practices (using 
a 
standard  site 
visit 
evaluation 
instrument) 
and medical record reviews 
(using 
standard 
medical record 
review 
form)  to 
evaluate 
quality 
of 
documentation 
and 
to 
detect 
fraudulent 
billing 
and 
up-coding. 
(iv) 
investigation, review, 
and reporting 
of 
patient complaints 
and 
grievances. 
(v) 
systems 
of 
review 
and reporting

Annex 
4: 
Cost 
Benefit Analysis Summary 
BRAZIL:  Bahia Health System 
Reform Project 
This 
annex presents 
the 
summary 
results 
of 
the cost-benefit analysis 
of 
the 
Health 
Project 
of 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia, 
Brazil, 
based 
upon 
the 
program's 
costs 
and 
the measurable 
economic 
benefits 
flowing 
from 
the 
successful 
implementation 
of 
the 
proposed program. 
The 
project 
will 
potentially 
save 
over 
16,000 
lives 
through 
improvements 
in 
the 
health 
care 
system 
and reductions 
in 
disability 
and 
mortality 
will 
contribute 
over 
400,000 
productive 
life 
years 
(DALYs) 
to 
the 
economy. 
In 
addition 
to 
the 
project 
costs 
of 
about 
$167 
million, 
the 
analysis 
includes 
the 
investment 
costs 
and 
recurrent 
expenditures 
related 
to 
improving 
access 
to 
primary 
health 
care 
services, 
strengthening emergency 
services, 
and 
restructuring 
the 
hospital 
network. 
In 
summary, 
the 
project 
would 
yield 
a present 
value 
of 
net 
benefits,  after 
investment 
and 
recurrent 
costs, 
of 
over 
$138.5 
million 
and produce 
an estimated 
internal 
rate 
of 
return 
(IRR) 
above 
84% 
over ten 
years. 
The 
Bahia health 
Sector 
Reform 
Project 
will 
produce 
substantial 
savings 
for 
the 
sector, 
primarily 
through 
reductions 
in 
hospitalization 
costs. 
The 
cost-benefit 
analysis, 
which 
gives  the 
project 
a 
BenefitKOst 
Index 
of 
3.84, 
will 
yield 
significant health 
benefits 
to 
the 
population 
and economic 
benefits 
to 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia. 
Projected recurrent 
expenditures 
generated 
by 
the 
project 
and the 
estimates 
of 
future health 
care 
spending 
show 
that 
the 
project 
would 
be 
financially 
sustainable. 
[For projects with benefits 
that 
are measured 
in 
monetary 
terms] 
Benefits: 
Present 
Value 
of 
Flows 
Fiscal 
Impact 
Economic 
Financial 
Analysis 
Analysis' 
Taxes 
Subsidies 
394.0 
M 
costs: 
167.0 
M 
Net 
Benefits: 
138.5 
M 
IRR: 
85% 
If 
the 
difference 
between 
the 
present 
value 
of 
financial 
and 
economic 
flows 
is 
large 
and 
cannot 
be 
explained 
by 
taxes 
and 
subsidies, a 
brief 
explanation 
of 
the 
difference 
is 
warranted, 
e.g. 
"The 
value 
of 
financial 
benefits 
is 
less 
than 
that 
of 
economic benefits 
because 
of 
controls 
on 
electricity 
tariffs." 
Summary 
of 
Benefits 
and 
Costs: 
The 
cost-benefit analysis 
distributes 
the 
benefits 
over 
this 
time horizon 
into 
two 
major 
groups: 
direct 
and 
indirect 
benefits. 
The 
direct 
benefits 
are 
the 
expected 
tangible 
benefits 
accruable 
to 
the 
project 
from 
the 
reduced 
hospital 
stays, 
eliminating 
unnecessary 
discharges, 
reducing 
consultation 
and 
from 
the 
restructuring and 
downsizing 
of 
hospital facilities. 
Indirect 
benefits 
are 
related 
to 
the 
potential 
life 
years 
saved 
by 
the 
project and 
the 
economic 
and financial value 
of 
increasing 
productivity. The 
cost-benefit 
analysis 
assumes 
that 
the 
strengthening 
and 
changes 
in 
the 
health 
care 
system 
will 
be 
targeted 
to 
4.9 
million 
people, 
equivalent 
to 
38 percent 
of 
Bahia's 
population 
and that 
a 
ten 
year 
period 
is 
required 
to 
reflect 
the 
flow 
of 
costs 
and 
benefits. 
The project 
will 
be 
divided into 
two 
phases. 
45 
Page  46
Among 
the 
most 
important 
direct 
benefits 
of 
the 
project 
are 
savings 
in 
hospitalizations. 
Savings 
in 
hospitalization 
costs 
will 
be 
accomplished through 
three 
sources 
of 
savings. 
First, 
the 
project 
will 
result 
in 
reduced hospitalizations 
due 
to 
the 
greater 
capacity 
to 
resolve 
medical 
needs 
at the 
local 
level, 
thus 
reducing 
referrals 
to 
hospitals. 
Second, 
the 
project 
will 
reduce 
risk 
factors 
in 
the target 
population 
that 
increase 
the 
demand 
for 
hospitalizations. 
The 
third 
source 
of 
savings 
comes 
from 
reduced administrative 
costs. 
Another 
probable 
source 
of 
savings, 
not 
explicitly 
quantified 
in 
this 
study, 
is 
the 
reduction 
in 
hospitalization 
costs 
due 
to 
increased 
efficiency 
of 
health 
networks 
as 
a 
result 
of 
improved 
management 
practices. 
The 
project 
will 
shift 
healthcare 
provision 
from 
expensive 
hospitals 
to 
more efficient 
and 
higher quality 
primary 
care 
centers. 
This 
shift 
will 
save 
over 
583 
thousand 
unnecessary 
hospitalizations 
over 
10 years, 
and 
produce 
a 
direct benefit 
of 
$1 
16 
million 
in 
nominal 
terms 
with 
a 
present 
value 
of 
$67.7 
million. 
The 
present value 
of 
savings 
in 
administrative 
costs 
is 
estimated 
at 
$4.86 
million 
for 
a 
total 
present value 
of 
direct 
benefits 
of 
$72.6 
million. 
The 
indirect 
benefits were 
estimated 
by 
analyzing 
the 
effect 
of 
Disability-Adjusted 
Life 
Years 
(DALYs), 
using 
the 
GDP 
per 
capita 
as 
the 
index 
for 
the 
productive value 
one year 
of 
an economically 
active citizen. 
The 
GDP 
per capita 
in 
the 
regions 
of 
interest 
was 
estimated at 
$3,171. 
The 
total 
benefits 
in 
terms 
of 
DALYs 
increase 
as 
the 
morbidity 
and 
mortality 
index 
improve 
over 
the 
first 
three 
years, 
when 
the target 
improvements 
are 
met. 
From 
that 
year 
onward 
the 
mortality index remain 
constant 
throughout 
the 
period 
of 
the 
study. 
It 
is 
estimated 
that 
the 
project 
will 
potentially 
contribute 
405,422 
productive 
life 
years 
(DALYs) 
to 
the 
economy 
and 
save 
over 
16,000 
lives 
as 
a 
result 
of 
applying 
the 
aforementioned 
assumptions 
to 
the 
pattern 
of 
mortality 
in 
the 
country. 
The reduction 
in 
mortality 
is 
taken 
as 
the 
base 
line 
assumption 
to 
calculate 
the 
indirect 
benefits 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
estimates 
of 
reduced 
mortality 
are 
based 
on 
conservative 
estimates 
based 
on 
historical 
trends 
in 
Bahia, 
intemational 
literature 
on 
cost- 
effectiveness, 
and 
expert's 
opinion. 
To 
calculate the 
indirect 
benefits, 
the discounted 
life 
years 
saved 
attributable 
to 
these 
reductions 
are 
then converted 
to 
monetary 
terms 
by 
using 
estimates 
of 
the 
discounted 
life 
time 
earnings 
lost 
from 
each 
death, 
as 
reflected 
by 
the 
use 
of 
average 
wage 
levels, 
adjusted 
for 
labor 
market 
participation, 
and 
the 
number 
of 
years 
of 
life 
lost for 
each 
death. 
The 
following 
tables 
summarize 
the 
results 
of 
the 
analysis 
for 
each 
of 
the 
two 
scenarios.

As 
shown 
in 
the 
table, 
benefits 
outweigh 
investments 
nearly 
four 
to 
one. 
Over 
ten 
years, 
the 
net 
present 
value 
of 
the 
project 
would yield 
benefits 
of 
approximately 
US$394 
million. 
With 
the 
exception 
of 
the 
first 
two 
years, 
the 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
generate 
annual 
savings 
to 
the 
system 
that  outstrip 
the 
investment 
cost, 
thereby 
paying 
for 
them. 
The 
internal 
rate 
of 
retum 
(IRR) 
for 
the 
project 
is 
estimated at 
85%, 
reflecting 
the 
extremely 
high 
benefits  relative 
to 
project 
costs. 
This 
ratio 
can 
be 
used 
to 
46 
Page  47
demonstrate 
the 
value 
of 
this 
project 
relative to 
other 
projects 
in 
other 
sectors; 
any 
number 
lower 
than 
the 
discount rate 
(10%) 
would 
imply 
that 
the 
project 
should 
not 
be 
carried out. 
In 
terms 
of 
the 
costs 
of 
implementation, 
the  analysis 
considers 
two 
basic 
elements: 
(i) 
the  external 
investment 
costs 
of 
the 
Bank 
project; 
and 
(ii) 
the 
recurrent 
costs. 
The 
external 
financing 
is 
disbursed over 
seven years 
as 
the 
project 
advances, 
with 
35% 
disbursed 
within 
the 
first 
two 
years, 
10% 
in 
the 
third 
year, 
and 
20%, 
15%, 
10% 
and 
10% 
in 
the 
following 
successive years, 
respectfully. 
The 
recurrent 
costs 
reach 
$4.1 
million 
per 
year 
after 
the 
fourth 
year, 
once 
the 
project 
is 
fully 
operational. 
This 
amount includes 
the 
recurrent 
and 
incremental 
costs 
of 
investments 
in 
infkastructure, 
equipment and 
maintenance, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
cost 
of 
increasing 
the 
coverage 
of 
basic 
health 
services 
through 
the 
primary 
care 
network.  Over 
a 
ten- 
year 
horizon, 
the 
present 
value 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
the 
project 
is 
$138.5 
million, 
while 
the 
present 
value 
of 
the 
total 
benefits 
is 
estimated 
at $532.5 
million, 
thus 
giving 
the 
project 
a 
Net 
Present 
Value 
of 
approximately 
$394 
million. 
The 
Net 
Present 
Value 
of 
the 
project 
over 
a 
six-year 
time 
frame 
is 
estimated 
at 
$154.4 
million, 
thus 
demonstrating 
the 
robustness 
of 
the 
project. 
Cost-Effectiveness 
The 
total 
discounted 
life 
years 
potentially 
saved 
through 
the 
project 
interventions 
are 
based 
on 
rough 
estimates 
of 
the 
potential reduction 
in 
infant 
and maternal 
mortality, 
and 
the 
discounted 
number 
of 
life 
years 
lost 
per 
death. 
While 
there 
are 
no 
detailed 
cost-effectiveness 
studies 
for 
Bahia 
that estimate the 
burden 
of 
disease 
or 
the 
number 
of 
disability 
adjusted 
life 
years 
lost, 
back 
of 
the 
envelop calculations 
are 
made 
using 
the 
mortality 
levels 
and 
coefficients 
on 
the 
ratio 
of 
DALYs 
per 
death 
taken 
from 
the 
Global 
Burden 
of 
Disease 
study. 
While 
this 
is 
not 
an 
exact  figure, 
it 
provides 
a 
guide 
for 
policymakers 
to 
estimate 
the project’s benefits. 
Using 
this 
methodology, 
it 
is 
estimated 
that 
the 
project 
would 
reduce the 
burden 
of 
disease 
by 
approximately 
465,000 
DALYs 
over 
the  10 
years. 
The 
overall 
average 
cost per 
DALY 
saved 
is 
US$342, 
while 
the 
cost 
per 
avoided 
death 
is 
$8,546. 
While 
these 
figures 
may 
seem 
high, 
they 
are 
compensated 
by 
the 
lifetime 
economic 
contribution 
of 
each 
individual. 
This 
is 
particularly 
evident 
in 
the 
case 
of 
reductions 
in 
infant mortality 
in 
a 
region 
where the 
average 
number 
of 
productive 
years 
is 
approximately 
50 
years. 
h 
addition, 
a 
substantial 
portion 
of 
the 
benefits 
will 
not 
be 
observed 
until 
after the 
study’s 
time 
frame. 
For 
example, 
avoided 
mortality 
for 
children 
under 
the 
age 
of 
one 
will 
not 
bear 
fruit 
in 
terms 
of 
economic 
activity 
for 
ten 
years 
or 
more. 
The 
average 
cost 
per 
discounted 
life 
year 
for 
the 
selected 
project 
interventions 
is 
in 
a 
range 
acceptable 
under 
the 
standards 
established 
in 
the 
1993 
WDR. 
This 
is 
in 
line 
with 
other 
project’s 
that 
promote 
system 
wide 
reforms, 
with 
limited 
interventions 
in 
primary 
care. 
Fiscal 
Impact 
and 
Sustainability 
The 
first 
step 
in 
the  analysis 
of 
the 
sustainability 
of 
the 
project 
was 
to 
establish  the 
base 
level 
expenditures 
prior 
to 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project. 
After 
this 
evaluation, 
estimates 
were made 
on 
the 
additional 
costs, 
which 
result 
from 
this 
project. 
In 
1997, 
total 
health 
expenditures 
in 
Brazil 
account 
for 
6.5% 
of 
the 
GDP, 
or  approximately $64 per 
capita. 
That 
same 
year 
public 
expenditures 
accounted 
for 
48.7% 
of 
total health 
care 
expenditures. 
For 
the 
current 
year 
(2000), 
the 
public 
health 
budget 
for 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia 
(including 
federal contributions) 
is 
R$650 
million 
(US$361 
million). 
The 
health 
expenditures 
of 
the 
Secretaria 
de Saude 
do 
Estado da 
Bahia 
(SESAB) 
account 
for 
1.7 
of 
the 
GDP 
of 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia. The 
federal govemment 
contributed 
$183.7 
million 
of 
the 
total 
SESAB 
budget 
for 
this 
year. 
It 
is 
important 
to 
note 
that 
there 
is 
a 
trend 
toward 
the 
decentralization 
of 
services 
provided 
through 
the 
SUS, 
from 
the 
federal 
level to 
the 
State 
and 
Municipal 
level. 
Besides 
this 
trend 
toward 
decentralization, 
the 
health 
sector 
is 
being 
reformed 
to 
create 
mechanisms 
to 
transfer 
funds 
from 
the 
federal government 
to 
State 
and 
Municipal 
governments. 
Current legislation 
indicates 
that 
the federal 
budget 
will 
increase 
the 
allocations 
to 
State 
and 
Municipal 
health 
budgets 
by 
7% 
47 
Page  48
in 
2000, 
and 
by 
12% 
and 
15% 
in 
2004 
for 
states 
and 
municipalities,  respectively. 
The 
projections 
of 
future 
public health 
expenditures 
are 
based 
on 
figures 
from 
1995 
and 
using 
real 
growth 
rates 
of 
8% 
for 
State 
budgets.

Based 
on 
the current projections 
for 
health 
expenditures, 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project 
will 
cost 
less 
than 
2% 
of 
the 
public 
expenditure 
on 
health 
care services 
in 
Bahia 
starting 
from 
the seventh 
year 
of 
the 
project, 
which 
is 
the 
starting 
point 
to 
analyze 
the 
sustainability 
of 
the 
project.  Recurrent 
costs 
attributable 
to 
recurrent 
costs 
from 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project 
represent 
additional 
costs 
of 
approximately 
5% 
above 
public 
health 
expenditures 
during 
the 
first 
six 
years 
of 
the project. 
These 
costs 
range 
from 
8% 
above 
base 
expenditures 
in 
the 
first 
year 
to 
a 
minimum 
of 
3% 
in 
the 
sixth 
year. 
In 
the 
following 
years, 
additional 
costs 
due 
to 
the 
project 
represent 
only 
an 
average 
of 
1% 
increase 
over 
normal 
expenditures. 
These 
figures 
indicate 
that 
the 
project 
is 
sustainable. 
In 
addition, 
the 
project 
is 
likely 
to improve 
future 
government 
tax 
revenues 
as 
a 
result 
of 
better 
productivity, 
reduced premature 
morbidity 
and 
mortality. 
However, 
its 
sustainability 
also 
depends 
on 
the 
political 
will 
of 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia to 
raise 
health 
care 
expenditures 
to 
the 
required levels 
in 
order 
to 
obtain 
and 
sustain the 
benefits 
of 
the 
project. 
Main 
Assumptions: 
The 
following 
parameters  are 
considered 
relevant 
in 
estimating 
the 
economic benefits 
of 
the 
proposed 
project: 
the 
length 
of 
the 
project 
horizon 
and 
the 
time 
to 
impact 
the 
health 
of 
the 
population, 
the 
size 
of 
the 
target 
population, 
the 
existing 
patterns 
of 
morbidity 
and 
mortality, 
the 
number 
of 
years 
of 
productive 
life 
added 
as 
a 
result 
of 
the percentage 
decrease 
in 
mortality, and 
the 
existing 
cost structure 
in 
the 
health 
sector. 
Given 
the 
medium-to-long-term effect 
of 
the 
changes, 
the 
estimates 
presented 
in 
terms 
of 
reduced 
morbidity, 
which 
assume 
a 
project 
horizon 
of 
only 
10 
years,  are 
very 
conservative. 
The 
additional 
costs 
of 
increasing 
the 
maternal 
and 
child 
health 
care services are 
reflected 
in 
the 
investment 
costs. 
The 
analysis 
of 
the 
Bahia 
Health 
Reform 
Project 
uses 
the 
following 
assumptions 
to 
measure the 
direct and 
indirect 
benefits: 
maternal 
mortality 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
50%; 
mortality 
from 
malnutrition 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
3 
1%; 
mortality 
from 
perinatal 
afflictions 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
14%; 
mortality 
from 
respiratory 
diseases 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
24%; 
mortality 
from 
CVD 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
25%; 
mortality 
from 
infectious 
diseases 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
22%; 
mortality 
from 
diabetes 
mellitus 
would 
be 
reduced 
by 
3 
1%; 
a 39% 
reduction over 
time 
in 
hospital 
discharges 
for 
infectious 
diseases; 
a 39% 
reduction 
in 
cardiovascular 
discharges; 
a 33% 
reduction 
in 
respiratory 
disease 
discharges; 
a 
20% 
reduction 
in 
discharges 
for 
maternal complications 
from 
childbirth; 
a 34% 
reduction 
in 
genitourinary 
discharges; 
and 
discount 
rate 
of 
10%.

In 
the 
following 
sections,  these 
assumptions 
will 
be used 
to 
evaluate 
the benefits 
of 
the 
project 
and 
to 
assess, 
from 
an 
economic 
perspective,  the 
impact 
of 
the 
project 
on 
public 
finances. 
All 
target 
reductions 
in 
morbidity 
and 
mortality 
would 
be 
reached after 
three years. 
Sensitivity analysis 
/ 
Switching 
values 
of 
critical 
items: 
While 
the above 
results 
are 
conservative, 
it 
is 
important 
to 
test 
the 
robustness 
of 
the 
results 
with 
regard 
to 
potential 
delays 
or 
reductions 
in 
benefits. 
This 
is 
done 
through 
the 
use 
of 
sensitivity 
analysis assuming 
delays 
of 
2 
and 
3 
years, 
as 
well 
as 
a 
reduction 
in 
benefits 
of 
20%, 
30% 
and 
40%.

BRAZIL:  Bahia Health System 
Reform Project 
A. 
Procurement 
Arrangements 
Procurement 
for 
the 
proposed 
project 
would  be 
carried  out 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
World 
Bank’s 
“Guidelines: 
Procurement  Under 
IBRD 
Loans 
and 
IDA 
Credits“, 
published 
in 
January 
1995 
(revised 
JanuarytAugust 
1996,  September 
1997 
and 
January 
1999); 
and 
“Guidelines: 
Selection 
and 
Employment 
of 
Consultants 
by 
World 
Bank 
Borrowers” 
published 
in 
January 
1997 (revised 
in 
September 
1997, 
January 
1999 and 
May 
2002); 
and 
the 
provisions 
stipulated 
in 
the 
Loan 
Agreement. 
The 
Bank’s 
Standard 
Bidding 
Documents 
and 
Standard Request 
for 
Proposals 
will 
be 
used 
for 
the 
Bank-financed 
ICB 
goods 
and 
consultant 
services, 
respectively. 
For 
NCB 
procurement 
of 
goods, 
PCU 
and 
the 
selected 
Municipalities 
will 
use 
the 
model 
standard 
bidding 
document 
issued 
by 
SEAIN. 
The 
pregiio 
and the 
electronic 
pregiio 
may be 
used 
as 
an 
alternative 
to 
shopping 
of 
off-the-shelf 
items 
in 
all 
project 
components. 
1. 
Procurement Responsibility. 
The 
Project 
implementation 
will 
require procurement 
of 
goods, 
hiring 
of 
consultants, 
training 
activities 
and small refurbishing 
of 
existing 
administrative 
and 
warehouses 
facilities 
or 
construction 
of 
small 
new 
health 
care 
facilities 
in 
the 
micro 
regions. 
The 
micro 
regions 
(microregi6es) 
are 
31 
state 
subdivisions 
grouping 
several 
municipalities 
with 
one 
of 
them 
acting 
as 
a 
leader. 
One 
of 
the 
objectives 
of 
the 
project 
is 
to 
strengthen 
these 
health 
administrations.  There 
are 
four 
types 
of 
implementing 
agencies 
under 
the 
proposed Project: 
at 
the central 
level, 
the 
Project 
Coordination 
Unit 
(PCU) 
through 
the 
Superintendencias 
and 
the 
Coordenapjo 
de 
Licitaq6es 
da 
Secretaria 
da 
Sazide 
(COLICI) 
will 
carry 
out 
hiring 
of 
consultants, 
and  procurement 
of 
larger 
information 
technology 
equipment, 
furniture  and 
materials; 
the 
procurement centralized 
at 
the central 
level 
is 
possible 
in 
the 
Project 
because 
SESAB 
has 
a 
good 
distribution 
network 
contracted 
out through national 
bidding; 
the 
31 
micro-regi6es 
that 
are 
subject 
to 
strengthening 
under 
the 
Project 
will 
hire 
services 
to 
host 
training 
activities, 
including 
rental 
of 
space 
and 
food 
services, 
in 
small 
amounts 
generally 
using 
administrative 
procedures 
of 
the 
regional 
DIRES 
of 
SESAB; 
the 
participating municipalities 
under 
the 
Basic 
Care 
Expansion 
subprojects 
component 
will 
carry 
out 
procurement activities 
to 
implement Basic 
Care 
Expansion 
Plans, 
including 
hiring 
of 
health 
workers 
and small 
investments. 
The 
disbursement 
mechanism 
to 
be 
used 
for 
these 
interventions 
is 
an 
agreement 
signed 
between 
SESAB 
and 
the 
beneficiary 
municipality 
(hereinafter 
“Term0 
de 
Compromisso”), 
with 
which 
it 
would 
be 
possible 
regulating 
and 
controlling 
the  procurement 
procedures 
as 
to 
be 
shopping, 
ICB, 
acceptable 
NCB 
and 
the 
use 
of 
Consultants 
Guidelines; 
and 
the 
state 
secretariat 
of 
administration 
SAEB 
and the 
superintendence 
of 
administrative 
civil 
works 
SUCAB 
will 
carry 
out 
the  procurement 
of 
vehicles 
and 
civil 
works, 
respectively, 
as 
this 
procurement 
is 
centralized 
in 
the 
state 
of 
Bahia. 
The 
PCU 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
overall 
project 
procurement 
activity, 
including 
compliance 
with 
procedures 
and

with

Bank. The

will 
hire

and

disciplines 
in

to

to

The

using 
terms 
of

agreed

Action

3. 
Procurement 
Plan. 
The 
PCU 
prepared 
a 
procurement 
plan 
for 
the 
tasks 
to 
be carried out 
during 
the 
project 
implementation. 
The procurement 
plans 
of 
the 
selected 
municipalities 
in 
the 
Basic 
Care 
Expansion 
component 
will 
form 
part 
of

Basic 
Care 
Expansion 
Plan 
and 
will 
be 
assessed 
by 
the 
PCU 
as 
they 
are 
prepared 
during 
the 
project implementation. 
The

plan 
was 
approved 
by

RF’A’s 
office 
and 
is 
51 
Page  52
in 
the 
project files. 
The

plans 
consist 
of: 
(i) 
procurement 
plan 
for

project’s 
goods, 
including 
contract 
packaging, 
applicable 
procedures 
and 
process scheduling; 
(ii) 
a 
consultant selection 
process 
plan 
for 
the 
project’s 
consultant 
services, 
including 
contract  packaging, 
applicable 
procedures, 
shortlists, 
selection criteria. 
The 
procurement 
plan 
and 
consultant selection 
process 
plan 
will 
be 
updated periodically, 
every 
6 
months. 
The 
plans 
include 
the 
(i) 
list 
of

completed, 
under 
execution, 
under 
procurement, 
to

in

upcoming

and, 
tentatively 
in 
the 
subsequent semester; 
(ii) 
costs 
of 
completed and under execution 
contracts, 
estimated 
costs 
for upcoming 
contracts; 
(iii) 
schedule 
of 
bidding; 
and 
(iv) 
particular 
methods 
of 
procurement 
of 
goods 
or 
selection 
of 
consultants.

In

files.

B. 
Financial 
Management 
and 
Disbursement Arrangements 
20. 
County 
Issues. 
The 
Country Financial Accountability 
Assessment 
(CFAA) 
concluded 
that 
Brazil 
has a 
well 
developed and centralized 
system 
of 
public 
financial 
management. 
Overall 
it 
is 
able 
to reliably 
track 
budget 
expenditures. 
However 
the 
institutional 
arrangements 
and 
processes 
are 
complex. 
While 
good 
results 
are 
achieved 
in 
terms 
of 
aggregate 
fiscal 
control, the government 
is 
less 
successful 
in 
achieving good 
expenditure 
prioritization 
and operational 
efficiency. 
The 
Brazilian 
system 
of 
public 
financial 
management 
yields  reliable 
information 
and 
adequate 
management 
and 
tracking 
of 
the  receipt 
and 
use 
of 
funds 
at 
national 
level, 
and 
is 
able 
to 
support 
Bank’s lending 
programs. 
2 
1. 
Strengths  and 
Weaknesses. 
Although 
SESAB 
has 
participated 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
several 
Federal projects 
and 
several 
of 
its 
staff 
has 
experience 
with 
Bank 
procedures, 
it 
has 
never been 
a 
Central 
Implementing 
Agency 
for 
Bank 
financed projects. 
This 
project 
is 
quite 
complex 
and 
it 
will 
demand 
a great 
deal 
of 
commitment 
from 
SESAB’s 
managers. 
An 
action 
plan 
was 
prepared 
based 
on 
the 
financial 
management 
assessment 
and 
SESAB 
has 
been 
working 
on 
the  actions 
agreed 
to 
develop 
the 
required 
management capacity. 
Additionally, 
there 
exists 
a 
home-grown

26. 
Information 
System. 
SESAB 
is 
not 
equipped 
with 
good information 
systems 
which would 
allow 
speeding 
up 
information 
among 
the 
Superintendencies 
and 
FESBA 
which 
this 
project 
could 
benefit from. 
In

has 
hired 
an 
IT 
firm 
to 
develop 
a 
financial,  procurement and

systems 
to 
improve

of

The

of

will

that the 
flow 
of 
information 
will 
work 
through 
the  system 
which 
will

at the 
PCU, 
and

installed 
at  each 
of 
the 
participating 
Superintendencies 
and 
DIRES. 
Each

these

and

will 
feed the system 
with 
their 
respective 
working 
plans 
where they 
will 
be 
able 
to monitor 
project 
implementation. 
SURAPS 
(the

responsible

of

Basic Care 
component) 
will 
also 
be

to

The

will 
be

to 
FESBA 
and 
procurement 
units 
so 
that 
they 
can 
also 
be 
involved 
in 
all 
phases 
of 
the 
processes 
of 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project activities 
and easily 
detect 
any bottleneck 
during 
the 
implementation 
processes. 
Hardcopies 
of

updated 
after each 
mission.

that

29. 
Accounting 
Policies 
and 
Procedures. 
FESBA 
reports 
directly 
to 
the 
Health 
Secretary 
and 
is 
the 
financial 
unit 
where 
all 
accounting and 
financial 
operations 
are 
practiced 
in 
SESAB, 
The 
conclusion 
is 
that 
FESBA 
is 
organized 
with 
experienced 
accounting 
staff 
and 
equipped 
with 
the 
minimum 
financial 
management 
control 
mechanisms. 
FESBA 
will 
benefit 
substantially 
from 
the 
financial 
management 
arrangements 
which 
consider 
basically 
the strengthening 
of 
the 
reporting 
information 
system 
and 
allocation 
of 
financial and 
administrative 
staff 
to respond 
to 
the projects 
needs. 
The 
budget 
financial 
execution 
occurs 
via 
the 
Integrated 
Financial 
And 
Accounting 
Systemn 
of 
Bahia 
(SICOF) 
when 
FESBA 
decentralizes 
the 
funds 
to 
the executing 
agencies (Superintendencies, 
DIRES, 
Hospitals 
and 
Reference 
Centers), 
so 
that 
these 
executing 
agencies 
can 
spend  the 
funds 
accordingly. 
The 
executing 
agencies 
of 
FESBA 
(e.g. 
Superintendencies, 
DES, 
Hospitals, 
Emergency 
Units 
and 
others) 
have 
the 
responsibility 
to 
account 
for 
the 
funds 
also 
via 
SICOF. 
The 
system 
complies 
with 
the 
Public 
Sector 
Budgetary 
Law, 
and 
has 
its 
structure 
based 
on 
a 
chart 
of 
accounts 
which 
allows 
FESBA 
to 
monitor 
the 
nature 
of 
the 
expenditures. 
FESBA 
keeps 
separate 
bank 
accounts 
for 
the 
sources 
of 
funds 
which 
are 
reconciled 
on 
a 
daily 
basis 
and 
provide 
instructions 
to 
the 
executing 
agencies 
by 
informing 
them 
from 
which 
bank 
account 
they can 
spend 
the 
funds 
from. 
FESBA 
is 
establishing 
information 
system mechanisms 
to 
allow 
the 
executing 
agencies 
to 
access 
directly  their 
bank 
accounts. 
This 
system 
can 
generate 
accounting reports 
(e.g. 
trial 
balances, 
general 
ledger), 
however  financial 
reports 
are 
prepared 
in 
excel 
spreadsheets. 
The  information 
system 
under 
development 
for 
the 
project 
will 
have an 
accounting 
module 
to 
generate 
FMRs 
and 
other 
financial 
reports 
as 
per 
their 
needs. 
FESBA 
has

the

and

The 
IPDPs 
The 
following 
prerequisites 
need 
to 
be 
met 
before

IPDP 
is 
prepared and 
implemented: 
1. 
The 
institutions 
responsible 
for 
government 
interaction 
with 
indigenous 
peoples 
should 
possess 
the 
social,  technical, 
and 
legal 
skills 
needed 
for  carrying 
out 
the 
proposed  development 
activities. 
Implementation 
arrangements 
should 
be 
kept 
simple. 
They 
should 
normally involve 
appropriate 
existing 
institutions, 
local 
organizations, 
and 
nongovernmental 
organizations 
(NGOs) 
with 
expertise 
in 
matters 
relating 
to 
indigenous 
peoples. 
2. 
Local 
patterns 
of 
social 
organization, 
religious 
beliefs, 
and 
resource 
use 
should 
be 
taken 
into 
account 
in 
the 
plan's 
design. 
3. 
The 
plan 
should 
avoid 
creating 
or 
aggravating 
the 
dependency 
of 
indigenous people 
on 
project 
entities. 
Planning 
should 
encourage 
early 
handover 
of 
project 
management 
to  local 
people 
whenever 
feasible. 
As 
needed, 
the 
plan 
should 
include 
general  education  and 
training 
in 
management 
skills 
for 
indigenous people 
from 
the 
onset 
of 
the 
project.

Institutional 
Capacity. 
The 
government

assigned

for

peoples 
are 
often 
weak. 
Assessing the 
track 
record, capabilities, 
and 
needs 
of 
those 
institutions 
is 
a fundamental 
requirement. 
Organizational 
issues 
that

to

through 
Bank 
assistance 
are 
the 
(i)

of

for

professional

(iii)

own

(iv)

and 
(v)

Implementation 
Schedule. 
An 
implementation 
schedule 
with 
benchmarks 
by 
which 
progress 
can 
be 
measured 
at 
appropriate 
intervals. 
Pilot 
programs 
are 
often 
needed 
to provide 
planning 
information 
for 
phasing 
the 
project 
component 
for 
indigenous 
peoples 
with 
the 
main 
investment. 
The 
plan 
should 
pursue 
the 
long-term 
sustainability 
of 
project 
activities 
subsequent 
to 
completion 
of 
disbursement. 
Monitoring 
and 
Evaluation. 
Independent 
monitoring 
capacities 
are 
usually 
needed 
when 
the 
institutions 
responsible 
for 
indigenous 
peoples 
have weak 
management histories. 
Monitoring 
by 
representatives 
of 
indigenous 
peoples' 
own 
organizations 
can 
be 
an 
efficient 
way 
for 
the 
project 
management 
to 
absorb 
the 
perspectives 
of 
indigenous beneficiaries and 
is 
encouraged 
by 
the 
Bank. 
Monitoring 
units 
should 
be 
staffed 
by 
experienced 
social 
science 
professionals, 
and 
reporting 
formats 
and 
schedules 
appropriate 
to 
the 
project's 
needs 
should 
be 
established. 
Monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
reports 
should 
be 
reviewed 
jointly 
by 
the senior management 
of 
the 
implementing 
agency 
or 
agencies 
and 
by 
the 
Bank. 
The 
evaluation reports should 
be 
made 
available 
to 
the 
public.

BRAZIL:  Bahia 
Health System 
Reform 
Project 
STATE 
OF 
BAHIA 
- 
STRATEGIC DEVELOPMENT 
PLAN 
Summary 
VISION 
STATEMENT 
BAHIA: 
A 
SOCIALLY 
JUST 
AND 
COHESIVE SOCIETY 
It 
is 
imperative 
to 
build 
a 
society that 
is 
capable 
of 
ascertaining 
the 
full 
rights 
of 
citizenship, 
including 
ample 
access 
to 
quality 
basic 
services 
and 
equipment 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
education, 
health, 
housing,  sanitation  and 
public 
safety, 
as 
well 
as 
information, 
culture,  sports 
and 
leisure. 
The 
strategy 
aims 
at 
improving 
income 
distribution 
coupled 
with 
a 
reduction 
in 
poverty 
and 
social 
exclusion 
indices. 
In 
a 
scenario 
of 
increasing alienation 
of 
part 
of 
the 
global 
society 
from 
the 
development 
process, 
it 
is 
crucial 
that 
Bahia 
set 
goals 
for 
reducing 
inequalities 
at 
the 
state 
level 
in 
order 
to 
ascertain 
social 
cohesion. 
BAHIA: 
A 
DIVERSIFIED 
AND 
COMPETITIVE 
ECONOMY 
The 
increasing 
competition 
among 
firms 
requires 
incentive  policies 
focused 
on 
a 
better 
harmonization 
of 
production 
chains, 
including 
improvements 
in 
technology 
and 
labor 
skills, 
as 
well 
as 
a 
reduction 
in 
the 
production 
costs 
of 
intermediary 
goods 
and 
the 
strengthening 
of 
inter- 
sectoral 
links 
by 
deepening and 
vertical integration 
of 
the 
existing production 
chains. 
In 
addition, 
it 
should 
be 
possible 
to 
innovate 
and develop 
a 
knowledge 
and 
infrastructure 
network 
that 
would 
reinforce 
the 
dynamic 
competitiveness 
of 
Bahia’s 
economy. 
It 
is 
envisioned 
that 
small 
and 
medium 
enterprises 
will 
become 
more 
competitive, 
generating 
new 
job 
opportunities and fostering 
the 
development 
of 
a 
network 
of 
intersectoral 
relations. 
BAHIA: 
A 
SPATIALY 
INTEGRATED 
SOCIETY 
The 
spatial integration 
of 
the society 
and 
the 
state 
economy 
is 
key 
for 
keeping 
it 
united. 
Given 
that 
the 
Salvador 
Metropolitan Region 
has 
already 
consolidated 
its 
position, 
it 
is 
important 
to 
pursue 
a 
deconcentration 
of 
economic 
activities. 
This 
implies 
the 
availability 
of 
an 
adequate 
level 
of 
infrastructure  and 
logistics 
for 
the 
different 
regions 
within 
the 
state 
and 
the 
position 
of 
these 
regions 
vis-a-vis 
the 
national 
and 
intemational 
trade 
flows. 
Medium 
size 
cities 
that 
are 
strategically important should 
be 
strengthened 
inasmuch 
as 
they 
serve 
the 
integration 
function for 
the 
regional 
economy 
by 
providing 
a 
more 
sophisticated 
set 
of 
goods 
and 
services, 
including 
public 
services. 
The 
integration 
of 
dynamic 
areas 
located 
near the 
perimeter 
of 
the 
state’s 
territory 
is 
key 
for 
addressing 
the development 
of 
the 
semi-arid 
region 
that 
is 
located 
in 
the 
central 
part 
of 
the 
state. 
The 
integration 
process 
would 
simultaneously 
create 
agglomeration 
economies 
and 
population 
densities 
that 
would 
more efficiently 
support 
the 
production 
and 
trade 
of 
goods 
and 
services.

Page  81
BAHIA: 
AN 
ENVIRONMENTALLY 
CLEAN 
STATE 
It 
is 
important 
that  a 
new 
environmental 
consciousness 
applied 
to 
business 
and 
public 
decision 
making 
processes 
be 
promoted 
that 
makes 
the 
appropriate 
use 
of 
natural 
resources 
for 
economic 
growth 
while 
minimizing 
the 
risks 
of 
environmental 
degradation. 
For 
the 
sake 
of 
future 
generations, 
Bahia 
has 
to 
embrace sustainable 
development 
model 
that 
is 
based 
on 
harmonious, 
socially 
just 
and 
environmentally 
sound 
growth. 
Modem 
society 
increasingly 
demands 
a 
new 
balance 
between 
human 
activities 
and the 
environment, 
with 
natural 
resources 
and cultural, artistic 
and 
architectural 
heritage 
becoming 
progressively 
more 
important. 
Consumers  themselves 
are 
demanding 
goods 
and 
services 
that 
are 
produced 
by 
environmentally 
clean 
processes 
and socially 
responsible 
companies. 
It 
is 
envisioned 
that 
technological 
progress 
will 
accompany  a 
greater 
conservation 
of 
the 
natural 
and 
cultural 
environment, 
making 
it 
possible 
to 
arrive 
at 
an 
intervention 
model 
that 
will 
help 
to 
preserve, 
recover, 
and 
make 
a 
rational 
and 
sustainable 
use 
of 
natural 
resources. 
It 
is 
also 
envisioned 
that 
instruments 
will 
be 
available 
for 
a 
more 
adequate 
management 
of 
environmental 
issues, 
especially 
those 
conceming 
water 
resources.

Re. 
: 
Proposed 
Bahia 
Health 
System Reform 
Project 
I 
am 
writing 
to 
confirm 
that 
the 
proposed 
Bahia Health 
System 
Reform 
Project 
is 
an integral 
part 
of 
the 
Strategic 
Development 
Plan for 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia, 
a 
summary 
of 
which 
is 
attached 
for 
information. 
While 
the 
Plan 
focuses 
on 
human 
development  and 
competitiveness, 
it 
aims 
at 
increasing Bahia’s 
Human 
Development 
Index 
(HDI-M) 
above 
the 
national 
average 
by 
the 
year 
2020. 
Hence, 
the above-referenced project, 
through 
its 
objectives 
of 
improving 
access 
of 
the 
poor 
to 
basic 
health 
services, 
the 
distribution 
of 
public 
services 
and 
the 
efficiency 
and 
quality 
of 
public 
expenditures, 
is 
fully 
consistent 
with 
Bahia’s 
Strategic 
Development 
Plan 
I 
take 
this 
opportunity 
to 
convey 
to 
you 
my 
most 
sincere 
regards. 
PAUL0

Mr. 
Vice 
President: 
I 
have 
the 
honor 
of 
sending 
you 
this 
letter 
outlining 
the 
policies 
relating to 
the 
reform 
of 
the 
Health 
System 
in 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia. 
The 
Secretariat 
of 
Health 
of 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia 
acts 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
basic 
legislation 
of 
the 
Sistema 
Unico 
de  Saude 
- 
SUS 
(Laws 
nos.  8.080/90 
and 
8.142/90, 
Decrees nos. 
99.438/90, 
NOBi96, 
NOAS 
2001/2002 and 
E.C. 
29/2000), 
formulating 
and 
carrying 
out 
the 
state 
health 
policy 
with 
the  objective 
of 
guaranteeing 
efficient 
and 
equitable 
universal 
access 
to 
services. 
For 
this, 
the 
following 
policy 
guidelines 
are 
proposed: 
0 
Policy-management 
reform 
of 
the 
Secretariat 
of 
Health 
of 
the 
State 
of 
Bahia 
- 
SESAB, 
through 
the 
modemization 
of 
its 
structure 
and 
work 
processes; 
89 
Page  90
0 
Regionalization 
in 
terms 
of 
quantitative-qualitative  expansion 
of 
access 
to 
basic 
health 
services 
and 
procedures 
of 
medium 
and 
high 
complexity, 
promoting 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
volume 
of 
resources 
in 
the 
sector 
and 
an 
equitable 
distribution 
between 
regions 
in 
the 
state, 
creating 
conditions 
for 
balancing 
the 
relation 
between 
supply 
and 
demand 
for 
health 
services, 
which 
would 
permit 
an 
effective 
organization 
of 
assistance 
provided, 
including 
correcting 
processes 
that 
lead 
to 
a 
fragmentation 
of 
services, 
loss 
of 
scale, 
weakening 
of 
central 
structures, 
inequities 
and 
inefficiencies; 
0 
Decentralization 
of 
health 
care 
actions and 
services, 
with 
integration 
between 
the 
municipal, 
state 
and 
federal 
spheres 
of 
the 
Sistema 
Unico 
de 
Saude 
- 
SUS 
and 
the 
consequent 
redefinition 
of 
functions and 
responsibilities 
of 
SESAB, 
principally 
for 
the 
technical cooperation 
and 
monitoring 
of 
Municipal Health 
systems; 
0 
Reverting 
to 
a service 
model, 
through 
the 
reorganization 
of 
health 
assistance, 
based 
on 
the 
establishment 
and 
strengthening 
of 
a 
high 
quality 
level 
of 
universal 
primary 
attention, 
which 
should 
be 
responsive 
to 
more 
than 
85% 
of 
the 
population’s health 
problems, 
leading 
to, 
as 
a result, a 
reduction 
in 
the demand 
for 
higher 
cost, 
higher 
complexity 
procedures. 
It 
is 
proposed 
that 
an 
efficient 
strategy 
be 
adopted 
to 
fulfill 
this 
objective, 
based 
on 
intemational 
experience 
- 
that 
has 
already 
been 
tested 
in 
Bahia 
- 
based 
on 
the performance 
of 
teams 
from 
the 
Family 
Health 
Program, 
with 
the 
consequent
